   <h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">
    Antarctica
   </h1>
   <div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">
    <div class="noprint" id="siteSub">
     From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia
    </div>

      <p>
       <b>
        Antarctica
       </b>
       (UK English
       <span class="nowrap">
        <span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt">
         <a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">
          /
          <span style="border-bottom:1px dotted">
           <span title="/æ/: 'a' in 'bad'">
            æ
           </span>
           <span title="'n' in 'nigh'">
            n
           </span>
           <span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">
            ˈ
           </span>
           <span title="'t' in 'tie'">
            t
           </span>
           <span title="/ɑː/: 'a' in 'father'">
            ɑː
           </span>
           <span title="'k' in 'kind'">
            k
           </span>
           <span title="'t' in 'tie'">
            t
           </span>
           <span title="/ɪ/: 'i' in 'kit'">
            ɪ
           </span>
           <span title="'k' in 'kind'">
            k
           </span>
           <span title="/ə/: 'a' in 'about'">
            ə
           </span>
          </span>
          /
         </a>
        </span>
       </span>
       or
       <span class="nowrap">
        <span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt">
         <a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">
          /
          <span style="border-bottom:1px dotted">
           <span title="/æ/: 'a' in 'bad'">
            æ
           </span>
           <span title="'n' in 'nigh'">
            n
           </span>
           <span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">
            ˈ
           </span>
           <span title="'t' in 'tie'">
            t
           </span>
           <span title="/ɑː/: 'a' in 'father'">
            ɑː
           </span>
           <span title="'t' in 'tie'">
            t
           </span>
           <span title="/ɪ/: 'i' in 'kit'">
            ɪ
           </span>
           <span title="'k' in 'kind'">
            k
           </span>
           <span title="/ə/: 'a' in 'about'">
            ə
           </span>
          </span>
          /
         </a>
        </span>
       </span>
       , US English
       <span class="nowrap">
        <span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt">
         <a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">
          /
          <span style="border-bottom:1px dotted">
           <span title="/æ/: 'a' in 'bad'">
            æ
           </span>
           <span title="'n' in 'nigh'">
            n
           </span>
           <span title="'t' in 'tie'">
            t
           </span>
           <span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">
            ˈ
           </span>
           <span title="/ɑːr/: 'ar' in 'far'">
            ɑːr
           </span>
           <span title="'k' in 'kind'">
            k
           </span>
           <span title="'t' in 'tie'">

           </span>
           <span title="/ə/: 'a' in 'about'">
            ə
           </span>
          </span>
          /
         </a>
        </span>
        <small class="nowrap">
         (
         <span class="unicode haudio">
          <span class="fn">
           <span style="white-space:nowrap">
            <a href="/wiki/File:En-us-Antarctica.ogg" title="About this sound">
             <img alt="About this sound" data-file-height="20" data-file-width="20" height="11" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/11px-Loudspeaker.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/17px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/22px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 2x" width="11"/>
            </a>
           </span>
           <a class="internal" href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/5/57/En-us-Antarctica.ogg" title="En-us-Antarctica.ogg">
            listen
           </a>
          </span>
         </span>
         )
        </small>
       </span>
       )
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5">
        <a href="#cite_note-5">
         [note 1]
        </a>
       </sup>
       is
       <a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">
        Earth
       </a>
       's southernmost
       <a href="/wiki/Continent" title="Continent">
        continent
       </a>
       . It contains the geographic
       <a href="/wiki/South_Pole" title="South Pole">
        South Pole
       </a>
       and is situated in the
       <a href="/wiki/Antarctic" title="Antarctic">
        Antarctic
       </a>
       region of the
       <a href="/wiki/Southern_Hemisphere" title="Southern Hemisphere">
        Southern Hemisphere
       </a>
       , almost entirely south of the
       <a href="/wiki/Antarctic_Circle" title="Antarctic Circle">
        Antarctic Circle
       </a>
       , and is surrounded by the
       <a href="/wiki/Southern_Ocean" title="Southern Ocean">
        Southern Ocean
       </a>
       . At 14,000,000 square kilometres (5,400,000 square miles), it is the fifth-largest continent. For comparison, Antarctica is nearly twice the size of
       <a href="/wiki/Australia_(continent)" title="Australia (continent)">
        Australia
       </a>
       . About 98% of Antarctica is covered by
       <a href="/wiki/Ice" title="Ice">
        ice
       </a>
       that averages 1.9 km (1.2 mi; 6,200 ft) in thickness,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bedmap2_6-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Bedmap2-6">
         [5]
        </a>
       </sup>
       which extends to all but the northernmost reaches of the
       <a href="/wiki/Antarctic_Peninsula" title="Antarctic Peninsula">
        Antarctic Peninsula
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       Antarctica, on average, is the coldest, driest, and windiest continent, and has the highest average
       <a href="/wiki/Elevation" title="Elevation">
        elevation
       </a>
       of all the continents.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dnaclimate_7-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-dnaclimate-7">
         [6]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Antarctica is a
       <a href="/wiki/Desert" title="Desert">
        desert
       </a>
       , with annual
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Precipitation_(meteorology)" title="Precipitation (meteorology)">
        precipitation
       </a>
       of only 200 mm (8 in) along the coast and far less inland.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8">
        <a href="#cite_note-8">
         [7]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The temperature in Antarctica has reached
       <a href="/wiki/Vostok_Station#Climate" title="Vostok Station">
        −89.2 °C (−128.6 °F)
       </a>
       (or even −94.7 as measured from space
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9">
        <a href="#cite_note-9">
         [8]
        </a>
       </sup>
       ), though the average for the third quarter (the coldest part of the year) is −63 °C (−81 °F). Anywhere from 1,000 to 5,000 people reside throughout the year at the
       <a href="/wiki/Research_stations_in_Antarctica" title="Research stations in Antarctica">
        research stations
       </a>
       scattered across the continent. Organisms native to Antarctica include many types of
       <a href="/wiki/Algae" title="Algae">
        algae
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Bacteria" title="Bacteria">
        bacteria
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Fungus" title="Fungus">
        fungi
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Plant" title="Plant">
        plants
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Protist" title="Protist">
        protista
       </a>
       , and certain
       <a href="/wiki/Animal" title="Animal">
        animals
       </a>
       , such as
       <a href="/wiki/Mite" title="Mite">
        mites
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Nematode" title="Nematode">
        nematodes
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Penguin" title="Penguin">
        penguins
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Pinniped" title="Pinniped">
        seals
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Tardigrade" title="Tardigrade">
        tardigrades
       </a>
       . Vegetation, where it occurs, is
       <a href="/wiki/Tundra" title="Tundra">
        tundra
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       Although myths and speculation about a
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Terra_Australis" title="Terra Australis">
         Terra Australis
        </a>
       </i>
       ("Southern Land") date back to antiquity, Antarctica is noted as the last region on Earth in recorded history to be discovered and colonised by humans, unsighted until 1820 when the
       <a href="/wiki/List_of_Russian_explorers" title="List of Russian explorers">
        Russian expedition
       </a>
       of
       <a href="/wiki/Fabian_Gottlieb_von_Bellingshausen" title="Fabian Gottlieb von Bellingshausen">
        Fabian Gottlieb von Bellingshausen
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Mikhail_Lazarev" title="Mikhail Lazarev">
        Mikhail Lazarev
       </a>
       on
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Vostok_(sloop-of-war)" title="Vostok (sloop-of-war)">
         Vostok
        </a>
       </i>
       and
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Mirny_(sloop-of-war)" title="Mirny (sloop-of-war)">
         Mirny
        </a>
       </i>
       sighted the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fimbul_ice_shelf" title="Fimbul ice shelf">
        Fimbul ice shelf
       </a>
       . The continent, however, remained largely neglected for the rest of the 19th century because of its hostile environment, lack of easily accessible resources, and isolation. In 1895, the first confirmed landing was conducted by a team of Norwegians.
      </p>
      <p>
       Antarctica is a
       <i>
        de facto
       </i>
       <a href="/wiki/Condominium_(international_law)" title="Condominium (international law)">
        condominium
       </a>
       , governed by parties to the
       <a href="/wiki/Antarctic_Treaty_System" title="Antarctic Treaty System">
        Antarctic Treaty System
       </a>
       that have consulting status. Twelve countries signed the Antarctic Treaty in 1959, and thirty-eight have signed it since then. The treaty prohibits military activities and mineral mining, prohibits nuclear explosions and nuclear waste disposal, supports scientific research, and protects the continent's
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ecozone" title="Ecozone">
        ecozone
       </a>
       . Ongoing experiments are conducted by more than 4,000 scientists from many nations.
      </p>
      <p>
      </p>
      <div class="toc" id="toc">
       <div class="toctitle" dir="ltr" lang="en">
        <h2>
         Contents
        </h2>
       </div>
       <ul>
        <li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-1">
         <a href="#Etymology">
          <span class="tocnumber">
           1
          </span>
          <span class="toctext">
           Etymology
          </span>
         </a>
        </li>

      <p>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology">
        Etymology
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Adelie_Penguins_on_iceberg.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3744" data-file-width="5616" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/Adelie_Penguins_on_iceberg.jpg/220px-Adelie_Penguins_on_iceberg.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/Adelie_Penguins_on_iceberg.jpg/330px-Adelie_Penguins_on_iceberg.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/Adelie_Penguins_on_iceberg.jpg/440px-Adelie_Penguins_on_iceberg.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Adelie_Penguins_on_iceberg.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Adelie_penguins" title="Adelie penguins">
          Adelie penguins
         </a>
         in Antarctica
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       The name
       <i>
        Antarctica
       </i>
       is the
       <a href="/wiki/Romanization_of_Greek" title="Romanization of Greek">
        romanised
       </a>
       version of the
       <a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">
        Greek
       </a>
       compound word ἀνταρκτική (
       <i>
        antarktiké
       </i>
       ), feminine of
       <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E1%BC%80%CE%BD%CF%84%CE%B1%CF%81%CE%BA%CF%84%CE%B9%CE%BA%CF%8C%CF%82" title="wikt:ἀνταρκτικός">
        ἀνταρκτικός
       </a>
       (
       <i>
        antarktikós
       </i>
       ),
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10">
        <a href="#cite_note-10">
         [9]
        </a>
       </sup>
       meaning "opposite to the
       <a href="/wiki/Arctic" title="Arctic">
        Arctic
       </a>
       ", "opposite to the north".
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11">
        <a href="#cite_note-11">
         [10]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       <a href="/wiki/Aristotle" title="Aristotle">
        Aristotle
       </a>
       wrote in his book
       <i>
        Meteorology
       </i>
       about an
       <i>
        Antarctic region
       </i>
       in c. 350 B.C.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12">
        <a href="#cite_note-12">
         [11]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <a href="/wiki/Marinus_of_Tyre" title="Marinus of Tyre">
        Marinus of Tyre
       </a>
       reportedly used the name in his unpreserved world map from the 2nd century A.D. The
       <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Rome" title="Ancient Rome">
        Roman
       </a>
       authors
       <a href="/wiki/Gaius_Julius_Hyginus" title="Gaius Julius Hyginus">
        Hyginus
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Apuleius" title="Apuleius">
        Apuleius
       </a>
       (1–2 centuries A.D.) used for the South Pole the romanised Greek name
       <i>
        polus antarcticus,
       </i>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13">
        <a href="#cite_note-13">
         [12]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14">
        <a href="#cite_note-14">
         [13]
        </a>
       </sup>
       from which derived the
       <a href="/wiki/Old_French" title="Old French">
        Old French
       </a>
       <i>
        pole antartike
       </i>
       (modern
       <i>
        pôle antarctique
       </i>
       ) attested in 1270, and from there the
       <a href="/wiki/Middle_English" title="Middle English">
        Middle English
       </a>
       <i>
        pol antartik
       </i>
       in a 1391 technical treatise by
       <a href="/wiki/Geoffrey_Chaucer" title="Geoffrey Chaucer">
        Geoffrey Chaucer
       </a>
       (modern
       <i>
        Antarctic Pole
       </i>
       ).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15">
        <a href="#cite_note-15">
         [14]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Before acquiring its present geographical connotations, the term was used for other locations that could be defined as "opposite to the north". For example, the short-lived French colony established in
       <a href="/wiki/Brazil" title="Brazil">
        Brazil
       </a>
       in the 16th century was called "
       <a href="/wiki/France_Antarctique" title="France Antarctique">
        France Antarctique
       </a>
       ".
      </p>
      <p>
       The first formal use of the name "Antarctica" as a continental name in the 1890s is attributed to the Scottish
       <a href="/wiki/Cartography" title="Cartography">
        cartographer
       </a>
       <a href="/wiki/John_George_Bartholomew" title="John George Bartholomew">
        John George Bartholomew
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16">
        <a href="#cite_note-16">
         [15]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="History_of_exploration">
        History of exploration
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/History_of_Antarctica" title="History of Antarctica">
        History of Antarctica
       </a>
      </div>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       See also:
       <a href="/wiki/List_of_Antarctic_expeditions" title="List of Antarctic expeditions">
        List of Antarctic expeditions
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Women_in_Antarctica" title="Women in Antarctica">
        Women in Antarctica
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       Antarctica has no indigenous population and there is no evidence that it was seen by humans until the 19th century. However, belief in the existence of a
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Terra_Australis" title="Terra Australis">
         Terra Australis
        </a>
       </i>
       —a vast continent in the far south of the globe to "balance" the northern lands of Europe, Asia and
       <a href="/wiki/North_Africa" title="North Africa">
        North Africa
       </a>
       —had existed since the times of
       <a href="/wiki/Ptolemy" title="Ptolemy">
        Ptolemy
       </a>
       (1st century AD), who suggested the idea to preserve the
       <a href="/wiki/Symmetry" title="Symmetry">
        symmetry
       </a>
       of all known
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Landmass" title="Landmass">
        landmasses
       </a>
       in the world. Even in the late 17th century, after explorers had found that South America and Australia were not part of the fabled "Antarctica", geographers believed that the continent was much larger than its actual size.
      </p>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:James_Weddell_Expedition.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="158" data-file-width="300" height="116" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/James_Weddell_Expedition.jpg/220px-James_Weddell_Expedition.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/8/87/James_Weddell_Expedition.jpg 1.5x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:James_Weddell_Expedition.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Painting of
         <a href="/wiki/James_Weddell" title="James Weddell">
          James Weddell
         </a>
         's second expedition in 1823, depicting the brig
         <i>
          Jane
         </i>
         and the cutter
         <i>
          Beaufroy
         </i>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       Integral to the story of the origin of the name "Antarctica" is how it was not named
       <i>
        Terra Australis
       </i>
       —this name was
       <a href="/wiki/Australia#Etymology" title="Australia">
        given to Australia instead
       </a>
       , and it was because of a mistake made by people who decided that a significant landmass would not be found farther south than Australia. Explorer
       <a href="/wiki/Matthew_Flinders" title="Matthew Flinders">
        Matthew Flinders
       </a>
       , in particular, has been credited with popularising the transfer of the name
       <i>
        Terra Australis
       </i>
       to Australia. He justified the titling of his book
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/A_Voyage_to_Terra_Australis" title="A Voyage to Terra Australis">
         A Voyage to Terra Australis
        </a>
       </i>
       (1814) by writing in the introduction:
      </p>
      <blockquote class="templatequote">
       <p>
        There is no probability, that any other detached body of land, of nearly equal extent, will ever be found in a more southern latitude; the name Terra Australis will, therefore, remain descriptive of the geographical importance of this country and of its situation on the globe: it has antiquity to recommend it; and, having no reference to either of the two claiming nations, appears to be less objectionable than any other which could have been selected.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17">
         <a href="#cite_note-17">
          [16]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </p>
      </blockquote>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:262px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bellingshausen-fr.svg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="621" data-file-width="503" height="321" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Bellingshausen-fr.svg/260px-Bellingshausen-fr.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Bellingshausen-fr.svg/390px-Bellingshausen-fr.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Bellingshausen-fr.svg/520px-Bellingshausen-fr.svg.png 2x" width="260"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Bellingshausen-fr.svg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         The First Russian Antarctic expedition 1819–1821.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       European maps continued to show this hypothesised land until Captain
       <a href="/wiki/James_Cook" title="James Cook">
        James Cook
       </a>
       's ships,
       <a href="/wiki/HMS_Resolution_(1771)" title="HMS Resolution (1771)">
        HMS
        <i>
         Resolution
        </i>
       </a>
       and
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/HMS_Adventure_(1771)" title="HMS Adventure (1771)">
         Adventure
        </a>
       </i>
       , crossed the Antarctic Circle on 17 January 1773, in December 1773 and again in January 1774.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18">
        <a href="#cite_note-18">
         [17]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Cook came within about 120 km (75 mi) of the Antarctic coast before retreating in the face of field ice in January 1773.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19">
        <a href="#cite_note-19">
         [18]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The first confirmed sighting of Antarctica can be narrowed down to the crews of ships captained by three individuals.
      </p>
      <p>
       According to various organisations (the
       <a href="/wiki/National_Science_Foundation" title="National Science Foundation">
        National Science Foundation
       </a>
       ,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20">
        <a href="#cite_note-20">
         [19]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <a href="/wiki/NASA" title="NASA">
        NASA
       </a>
       ,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21">
        <a href="#cite_note-21">
         [20]
        </a>
       </sup>
       the
       <a href="/wiki/University_of_California,_San_Diego" title="University of California, San Diego">
        University of California, San Diego
       </a>
       ,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22">
        <a href="#cite_note-22">
         [21]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <a href="/wiki/Arctic_and_Antarctic_Museum" title="Arctic and Antarctic Museum">
        Russian State Museum of the Arctic and Antarctic
       </a>
       ,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23">
        <a href="#cite_note-23">
         [22]
        </a>
       </sup>
       among others),
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24">
        <a href="#cite_note-24">
         [23]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25">
        <a href="#cite_note-25">
         [24]
        </a>
       </sup>
       ships captained by three men sighted Antarctica or its ice shelf in 1820: von Bellingshausen (a captain in the
       <a href="/wiki/Imperial_Russian_Navy" title="Imperial Russian Navy">
        Imperial Russian Navy
       </a>
       ),
       <a href="/wiki/Edward_Bransfield" title="Edward Bransfield">
        Edward Bransfield
       </a>
       (a captain in the
       <a href="/wiki/Royal_Navy" title="Royal Navy">
        Royal Navy
       </a>
       ), and
       <a href="/wiki/Nathaniel_Palmer" title="Nathaniel Palmer">
        Nathaniel Palmer
       </a>
       (a
       <a href="/wiki/Seal_hunting" title="Seal hunting">
        sealer
       </a>
       out of
       <a href="/wiki/Stonington,_Connecticut" title="Stonington, Connecticut">
        Stonington, Connecticut
       </a>
       ).
      </p>
      <p>
       The First Russian Antarctic expedition led by
       <a href="/wiki/Fabian_Gottlieb_von_Bellingshausen" title="Fabian Gottlieb von Bellingshausen">
        Bellingshausen
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Mikhail_Lazarev" title="Mikhail Lazarev">
        Mikhail Lazarev
       </a>
       on the 985-ton
       <a href="/wiki/Sloop-of-war" title="Sloop-of-war">
        sloop-of-war
       </a>
       <a href="/wiki/Vostok_(sloop-of-war)" title="Vostok (sloop-of-war)">
        Vostok
       </a>
       ("East") and the 530-ton support vessel
       <a href="/wiki/Mirny_(sloop-of-war)" title="Mirny (sloop-of-war)">
        Mirny
       </a>
       ("Peaceful") reached a point within 32 km (20 mi) from
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Queen_Maud%27s_Land" title="Queen Maud's Land">
        Queen Maud's Land
       </a>
       and recorded the sight of an ice shelf at
       <span class="plainlinks nourlexpansion">
        <a class="external text" href="//tools.wmflabs.org/geohack/geohack.php?pagename=Antarctica&amp;params=69_21_28_S_2_14_50_W_">
         <span class="geo-default">
          <span class="geo-dms" title="Maps, aerial photos, and other data for this location">
           <span class="latitude">
            69°21′28″S
           </span>
           <span class="longitude">
            2°14′50″W
           </span>
          </span>
         </span>
         <span class="geo-multi-punct">
          ﻿ / ﻿
         </span>
         <span class="geo-nondefault">
          <span class="geo-dec" title="Maps, aerial photos, and other data for this location">
           69.35778°S 2.24722°W
          </span>
          <span style="display:none">
           ﻿ /
           <span class="geo">
            -69.35778; -2.24722
           </span>
          </span>
         </span>
        </a>
       </span>
       ,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tammiksaar_26-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-tammiksaar-26">
         [25]
        </a>
       </sup>
       on 27 January,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27">
        <a href="#cite_note-27">
         [26]
        </a>
       </sup>
       which became known as the
       <a href="/wiki/Fimbul_Ice_Shelf" title="Fimbul Ice Shelf">
        Fimbul ice shelf
       </a>
       . This happened three days before Bransfield sighted land, and ten months before Palmer did so in November 1820. The first documented landing on Antarctica was by the American sealer
       <a href="/wiki/John_Davis_(sealer)" title="John Davis (sealer)">
        John Davis
       </a>
       , apparently at
       <a href="/wiki/Hughes_Bay" title="Hughes Bay">
        Hughes Bay
       </a>
       , near Cape Charles, in
       <a href="/wiki/West_Antarctica" title="West Antarctica">
        West Antarctica
       </a>
       on 7 February 1821, although some historians dispute this claim.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jb04AA_28-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-jb04AA-28">
         [27]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MNP-CCJ92_29-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-MNP-CCJ92-29">
         [28]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The first recorded and confirmed landing was at Cape Adair in 1895.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RICpseb01_30-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-RICpseb01-30">
         [29]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:TheSouthernParty.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1029" data-file-width="1386" height="163" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4a/TheSouthernParty.jpg/220px-TheSouthernParty.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4a/TheSouthernParty.jpg/330px-TheSouthernParty.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4a/TheSouthernParty.jpg/440px-TheSouthernParty.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:TheSouthernParty.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         <a href="/wiki/Nimrod_Expedition" title="Nimrod Expedition">
          Nimrod Expedition
         </a>
         South Pole Party (left to right):
         <a href="/wiki/Frank_Wild" title="Frank Wild">
          Wild
         </a>
         ,
         <a href="/wiki/Ernest_Shackleton" title="Ernest Shackleton">
          Shackleton
         </a>
         ,
         <a href="/wiki/Eric_Marshall" title="Eric Marshall">
          Marshall
         </a>
         and
         <a href="/wiki/Jameson_Adams" title="Jameson Adams">
          Adams
         </a>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Aan_de_Zuidpool_-_p1913-160.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="321" data-file-width="486" height="145" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/Aan_de_Zuidpool_-_p1913-160.jpg/220px-Aan_de_Zuidpool_-_p1913-160.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/Aan_de_Zuidpool_-_p1913-160.jpg/330px-Aan_de_Zuidpool_-_p1913-160.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/Aan_de_Zuidpool_-_p1913-160.jpg/440px-Aan_de_Zuidpool_-_p1913-160.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Aan_de_Zuidpool_-_p1913-160.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         <a href="/wiki/Roald_Amundsen" title="Roald Amundsen">
          Roald Amundsen
         </a>
         and his crew looking at the Norwegian flag at the
         <a href="/wiki/South_Pole" title="South Pole">
          South Pole
         </a>
         , 1911
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Base_Dumont_d%27Urville_-_Dumont_d%27Urville_station.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="533" data-file-width="800" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/Base_Dumont_d%27Urville_-_Dumont_d%27Urville_station.jpg/220px-Base_Dumont_d%27Urville_-_Dumont_d%27Urville_station.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/Base_Dumont_d%27Urville_-_Dumont_d%27Urville_station.jpg/330px-Base_Dumont_d%27Urville_-_Dumont_d%27Urville_station.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/Base_Dumont_d%27Urville_-_Dumont_d%27Urville_station.jpg/440px-Base_Dumont_d%27Urville_-_Dumont_d%27Urville_station.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Base_Dumont_d%27Urville_-_Dumont_d%27Urville_station.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         <a href="/wiki/Dumont_d%27Urville_Station" title="Dumont d'Urville Station">
          Dumont d'Urville Station
         </a>
         , an example of modern human settlement in Antarctica
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       On 22 January 1840, two days after the discovery of the coast west of the
       <a href="/wiki/Balleny_Islands" title="Balleny Islands">
        Balleny Islands
       </a>
       , some members of the crew of the 1837–40 expedition of
       <a href="/wiki/Jules_Dumont_d%27Urville" title="Jules Dumont d'Urville">
        Jules Dumont d'Urville
       </a>
       disembarked on the highest islet
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31">
        <a href="#cite_note-31">
         [30]
        </a>
       </sup>
       of a
       <a href="/wiki/Dumoulin_Islands" title="Dumoulin Islands">
        group of rocky islands
       </a>
       about 4 km from
       <a href="/wiki/Cape_G%C3%A9od%C3%A9sie" title="Cape Géodésie">
        Cape Géodésie
       </a>
       on the coast of
       <a href="/wiki/Ad%C3%A9lie_Land" title="Adélie Land">
        Adélie Land
       </a>
       where they took some mineral, algae and animal samples.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32">
        <a href="#cite_note-32">
         [31]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       In December 1839, as part of the
       <a href="/wiki/United_States_Exploring_Expedition" title="United States Exploring Expedition">
        United States Exploring Expedition
       </a>
       of 1838–42 conducted by the
       <a href="/wiki/United_States_Navy" title="United States Navy">
        United States Navy
       </a>
       (sometimes called the "Ex. Ex.", or "the Wilkes Expedition"), an expedition sailed from
       <a href="/wiki/Sydney" title="Sydney">
        Sydney
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Australia" title="Australia">
        Australia
       </a>
       , into the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Antarctic_Ocean" title="Antarctic Ocean">
        Antarctic Ocean
       </a>
       , as it was then known, and reported the discovery "of an Antarctic continent west of the Balleny Islands" on 25 January 1840. That part of Antarctica was later named "
       <a href="/wiki/Wilkes_Land" title="Wilkes Land">
        Wilkes Land
       </a>
       ", a name it retains to this day.
      </p>
      <p>
       Explorer
       <a href="/wiki/James_Clark_Ross" title="James Clark Ross">
        James Clark Ross
       </a>
       passed through what is now known as the
       <a href="/wiki/Ross_Sea" title="Ross Sea">
        Ross Sea
       </a>
       and discovered
       <a href="/wiki/Ross_Island" title="Ross Island">
        Ross Island
       </a>
       (both of which were named after him) in 1841. He sailed along a huge wall of ice that was later named the
       <a href="/wiki/Ross_Ice_Shelf" title="Ross Ice Shelf">
        Ross Ice Shelf
       </a>
       .
       <a href="/wiki/Mount_Erebus" title="Mount Erebus">
        Mount Erebus
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Mount_Terror_(Antarctica)" title="Mount Terror (Antarctica)">
        Mount Terror
       </a>
       are named after two ships from his expedition:
       <a href="/wiki/HMS_Erebus_(1826)" title="HMS Erebus (1826)">
        HMS
        <i>
         Erebus
        </i>
       </a>
       and
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/HMS_Terror_(1813)" title="HMS Terror (1813)">
         Terror
        </a>
       </i>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33">
        <a href="#cite_note-33">
         [32]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <a href="/wiki/Mercator_Cooper" title="Mercator Cooper">
        Mercator Cooper
       </a>
       landed in
       <a href="/wiki/East_Antarctica" title="East Antarctica">
        East Antarctica
       </a>
       on 26 January 1853.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34">
        <a href="#cite_note-34">
         [33]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       During the
       <a href="/wiki/Nimrod_Expedition" title="Nimrod Expedition">
        Nimrod Expedition
       </a>
       led by
       <a href="/wiki/Ernest_Shackleton" title="Ernest Shackleton">
        Ernest Shackleton
       </a>
       in 1907, parties led by
       <a href="/wiki/Edgeworth_David" title="Edgeworth David">
        Edgeworth David
       </a>
       became the first to climb Mount Erebus and to reach the
       <a href="/wiki/South_Magnetic_Pole" title="South Magnetic Pole">
        South Magnetic Pole
       </a>
       .
       <a href="/wiki/Douglas_Mawson" title="Douglas Mawson">
        Douglas Mawson
       </a>
       , who assumed the leadership of the Magnetic Pole party on their perilous return, went on to lead several expeditions until retiring in 1931.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35">
        <a href="#cite_note-35">
         [34]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In addition, Shackleton himself and three other members of his expedition made several firsts in December 1908 – February 1909: they were the first humans to traverse the Ross Ice Shelf, the first to traverse the
       <a href="/wiki/Transantarctic_Mountains" title="Transantarctic Mountains">
        Transantarctic Mountains
       </a>
       (via the
       <a href="/wiki/Beardmore_Glacier" title="Beardmore Glacier">
        Beardmore Glacier
       </a>
       ), and the first to set foot on the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/South_Polar_Plateau" title="South Polar Plateau">
        South Polar Plateau
       </a>
       . An
       <a href="/wiki/Amundsen%27s_South_Pole_expedition" title="Amundsen's South Pole expedition">
        expedition led by Norwegian polar explorer Roald Amundsen
       </a>
       from the ship
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Fram" title="Fram">
         Fram
        </a>
       </i>
       became the first to reach the geographic South Pole on 14 December 1911, using a route from the
       <a href="/wiki/Bay_of_Whales" title="Bay of Whales">
        Bay of Whales
       </a>
       and up the
       <a href="/wiki/Axel_Heiberg_Glacier" title="Axel Heiberg Glacier">
        Axel Heiberg Glacier
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36">
        <a href="#cite_note-36">
         [35]
        </a>
       </sup>
       One month later, the doomed
       <a href="/wiki/Terra_Nova_Expedition" title="Terra Nova Expedition">
        Scott Expedition
       </a>
       reached the pole.
      </p>
      <p>
       <a href="/wiki/Richard_E._Byrd" title="Richard E. Byrd">
        Richard E. Byrd
       </a>
       led several voyages to the Antarctic by plane in the 1930s and 1940s. He is credited with implementing mechanised land
       <a href="/wiki/Transport_in_Antarctica" title="Transport in Antarctica">
        transport on the continent
       </a>
       and conducting extensive geological and biological research.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37">
        <a href="#cite_note-37">
         [36]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The first women to set foot on Antarctica did so in the 1930s with
       <a href="/wiki/Caroline_Mikkelsen" title="Caroline Mikkelsen">
        Caroline Mikkelsen
       </a>
       landing on an island of Antarctica in 1935,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-development.tas.gov.au_38-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-development.tas.gov.au-38">
         [37]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Ingrid_Christensen" title="Ingrid Christensen">
        Ingrid Christensen
       </a>
       stepping onto the mainland in 1937.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:5_39-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-:5-39">
         [38]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:32_40-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-:32-40">
         [39]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:12_41-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-:12-41">
         [40]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       It was not until 31 October 1956 that anyone set foot on the South Pole again; on that day a U.S. Navy group led by Rear Admiral
       <a href="/wiki/George_J._Dufek" title="George J. Dufek">
        George J. Dufek
       </a>
       successfully landed an aircraft there.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42">
        <a href="#cite_note-42">
         [41]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The first women to step onto the South Pole were Pam Young, Jean Pearson,
       <a href="/wiki/Lois_Jones_(scientist)" title="Lois Jones (scientist)">
        Lois Jones
       </a>
       , Eileen McSaveney, Kay Lindsay and Terry Tickhill in 1969.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43">
        <a href="#cite_note-43">
         [42]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The first person to sail single-handed to Antarctica was the
       <a href="/wiki/New_Zealand" title="New Zealand">
        New Zealander
       </a>
       <a href="/wiki/David_Henry_Lewis" title="David Henry Lewis">
        David Henry Lewis
       </a>
       , in 1972, in the 10-metre steel sloop
       <i>
        Ice Bird
       </i>
       .
      </p>
      <div style="clear:both;">
      </div>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Geography">
        Geography
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Geography_of_Antarctica" title="Geography of Antarctica">
        Geography of Antarctica
       </a>
      </div>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       See also:
       <a href="/wiki/Extreme_points_of_Antarctica" title="Extreme points of Antarctica">
        Extreme points of Antarctica
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/List_of_Antarctic_and_subantarctic_islands" title="List of Antarctic and subantarctic islands">
        List of Antarctic and subantarctic islands
       </a>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Antarctica.svg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="906" data-file-width="907" height="300" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/Antarctica.svg/300px-Antarctica.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/Antarctica.svg/450px-Antarctica.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/Antarctica.svg/600px-Antarctica.svg.png 2x" width="300"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Antarctica.svg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Labeled map of Antarctica
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       Positioned asymmetrically around the
       <a href="/wiki/South_Pole" title="South Pole">
        South Pole
       </a>
       and largely south of the Antarctic Circle, Antarctica is the southernmost continent and is surrounded by the
       <a href="/wiki/Southern_Ocean" title="Southern Ocean">
        Southern Ocean
       </a>
       ; alternatively, it may be considered to be surrounded by the southern
       <a href="/wiki/Pacific_Ocean" title="Pacific Ocean">
        Pacific
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_Ocean" title="Atlantic Ocean">
        Atlantic
       </a>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/Indian_Ocean" title="Indian Ocean">
        Indian Oceans
       </a>
       , or by the southern waters of the
       <a href="/wiki/World_Ocean" title="World Ocean">
        World Ocean
       </a>
       . There are a number of rivers and lakes in Antarctica, the longest river being the
       <a href="/wiki/Onyx_River" title="Onyx River">
        Onyx
       </a>
       . The largest lake,
       <a href="/wiki/Lake_Vostok" title="Lake Vostok">
        Vostok
       </a>
       , is one of the largest sub-glacial lakes in the world. Antarctica covers more than 14 million km
       <sup>
        2
       </sup>
       (5,400,000 sq mi),
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CIAfactbook-People_1-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-CIAfactbook-People-1">
         [1]
        </a>
       </sup>
       making it the fifth-largest continent, about 1.3 times as large as Europe. The coastline measures 17,968 km (11,165 mi)
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CIAfactbook-People_1-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-CIAfactbook-People-1">
         [1]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and is mostly characterised by ice formations, as the following table shows:
      </p>
      <table class="wikitable">
       <caption>
        <b>
         Coastal types around Antarctica
        </b>
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44">
         <a href="#cite_note-44">
          [43]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </caption>
       <tr>
        <th>
         Type
        </th>
        <th>
         Frequency
        </th>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
         <a href="/wiki/Ice_shelf" title="Ice shelf">
          Ice shelf
         </a>
         (floating ice front)
        </td>
        <td style="text-align:right;">
         44%
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
         Ice walls (resting on ground)
        </td>
        <td style="text-align:right;">
         38%
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
         Ice stream/outlet glacier (ice front or ice wall)
        </td>
        <td style="text-align:right;">
         13%
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
         Rock
        </td>
        <td style="text-align:right;">
         5%
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr style="text-align:right;">
        <td>
         Total
        </td>
        <td>
         100%
        </td>
       </tr>
      </table>
      <p>
       Antarctica is divided in two by the
       <a href="/wiki/Transantarctic_Mountains" title="Transantarctic Mountains">
        Transantarctic Mountains
       </a>
       close to the neck between the Ross Sea and the
       <a href="/wiki/Weddell_Sea" title="Weddell Sea">
        Weddell Sea
       </a>
       . The portion west of the Weddell Sea and east of the Ross Sea is called West Antarctica and the remainder East Antarctica, because they roughly correspond to the Western and Eastern Hemispheres relative to the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Greenwich_meridian" title="Greenwich meridian">
        Greenwich meridian
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Antarctica_surface.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="918" data-file-width="1026" height="197" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/Antarctica_surface.jpg/220px-Antarctica_surface.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/Antarctica_surface.jpg/330px-Antarctica_surface.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/Antarctica_surface.jpg/440px-Antarctica_surface.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Antarctica_surface.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Elevation coloured by relief height
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       About 98% of Antarctica is covered by the
       <a href="/wiki/Antarctic_ice_sheet" title="Antarctic ice sheet">
        Antarctic ice sheet
       </a>
       , a
       <a href="/wiki/Ice_sheet" title="Ice sheet">
        sheet of ice
       </a>
       averaging at least 1.6 km (1.0 mi) thick. The continent has about 90% of the world's ice (and thereby about 70% of the world's
       <a href="/wiki/Fresh_water" title="Fresh water">
        fresh water
       </a>
       ). If all of this ice were melted, sea levels would rise about 60 m (200 ft).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-howstuffworks_45-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-howstuffworks-45">
         [44]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In most of the interior of the continent,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Precipitation_(meteorology)" title="Precipitation (meteorology)">
        precipitation
       </a>
       is very low, down to 20 mm (0.8 in) per year; in a few "
       <a href="/wiki/Blue_ice_(glacial)" title="Blue ice (glacial)">
        blue ice
       </a>
       " areas precipitation is lower than mass loss by
       <a href="/wiki/Sublimation_(phase_transition)" title="Sublimation (phase transition)">
        sublimation
       </a>
       and so the local mass balance is negative. In the
       <a href="/wiki/McMurdo_Dry_Valleys" title="McMurdo Dry Valleys">
        dry valleys
       </a>
       , the same effect occurs over a rock base, leading to a desiccated landscape.
      </p>
      <p>
       <a href="/wiki/West_Antarctica" title="West Antarctica">
        West Antarctica
       </a>
       is covered by the
       <a href="/wiki/West_Antarctic_Ice_Sheet" title="West Antarctic Ice Sheet">
        West Antarctic Ice Sheet
       </a>
       . The sheet has been of recent concern because of the real, if small, possibility of its collapse. If the sheet were to break down,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sea_level_change" title="Sea level change">
        ocean levels
       </a>
       would rise by several metres in a relatively
       <a href="/wiki/Geologic_time_scale" title="Geologic time scale">
        geologically short
       </a>
       period of time, perhaps a matter of centuries. Several Antarctic
       <a href="/wiki/Ice_stream" title="Ice stream">
        ice streams
       </a>
       , which account for about 10% of the ice sheet, flow to one of the many
       <a href="/wiki/Ice_shelf#Antarctic_ice_shelves" title="Ice shelf">
        Antarctic ice shelves
       </a>
       : see
       <a href="/wiki/Ice-sheet_dynamics" title="Ice-sheet dynamics">
        ice-sheet dynamics
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       East Antarctica lies on the Indian Ocean side of the
       <a href="/wiki/Transantarctic_Mountains" title="Transantarctic Mountains">
        Transantarctic Mountains
       </a>
       and comprises
       <a href="/wiki/Coats_Land" title="Coats Land">
        Coats Land
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Queen_Maud_Land" title="Queen Maud Land">
        Queen Maud Land
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Enderby_Land" title="Enderby Land">
        Enderby Land
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Mac._Robertson_Land" title="Mac. Robertson Land">
        <abbr title="Macpherson">
         Mac
        </abbr>
        . Robertson Land
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Wilkes_Land" title="Wilkes Land">
        Wilkes Land
       </a>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/Victoria_Land" title="Victoria Land">
        Victoria Land
       </a>
       . All but a small portion of this region lies within the
       <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Hemisphere" title="Eastern Hemisphere">
        Eastern Hemisphere
       </a>
       . East Antarctica is largely covered by the
       <a href="/wiki/East_Antarctic_Ice_Sheet" title="East Antarctic Ice Sheet">
        East Antarctic Ice Sheet
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mount_Erebus_Aerial_2.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2048" data-file-width="3072" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/Mount_Erebus_Aerial_2.jpg/220px-Mount_Erebus_Aerial_2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/Mount_Erebus_Aerial_2.jpg/330px-Mount_Erebus_Aerial_2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/Mount_Erebus_Aerial_2.jpg/440px-Mount_Erebus_Aerial_2.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Mount_Erebus_Aerial_2.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         <a href="/wiki/Mount_Erebus" title="Mount Erebus">
          Mount Erebus
         </a>
         , an active volcano on
         <a href="/wiki/Ross_Island" title="Ross Island">
          Ross Island
         </a>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       <a href="/wiki/Vinson_Massif" title="Vinson Massif">
        Vinson Massif
       </a>
       , the highest peak in Antarctica at 4,892 m (16,050 ft), is located in the
       <a href="/wiki/Ellsworth_Mountains" title="Ellsworth Mountains">
        Ellsworth Mountains
       </a>
       . Antarctica contains
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_mountains_in_Antarctica" title="List of mountains in Antarctica">
        many other mountains
       </a>
       , on both the main continent and the surrounding islands. Mount Erebus on
       <a href="/wiki/Ross_Island" title="Ross Island">
        Ross Island
       </a>
       is the world's southernmost active volcano. Another well-known volcano is found on
       <a href="/wiki/Deception_Island" title="Deception Island">
        Deception Island
       </a>
       , which is famous for a giant eruption in 1970. Minor eruptions are frequent and lava flow has been observed in recent years. Other dormant volcanoes may potentially be active.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46">
        <a href="#cite_note-46">
         [45]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In 2004, a potentially active underwater volcano was found in the
       <a href="/wiki/Antarctic_Peninsula" title="Antarctic Peninsula">
        Antarctic Peninsula
       </a>
       by American and Canadian researchers.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47">
        <a href="#cite_note-47">
         [46]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Antarctica is home to more than 70 lakes that lie at the base of the continental ice sheet.
       <a href="/wiki/Lake_Vostok" title="Lake Vostok">
        Lake Vostok
       </a>
       , discovered beneath Russia's
       <a href="/wiki/Vostok_Station" title="Vostok Station">
        Vostok Station
       </a>
       in 1996, is the largest of these
       <a href="/wiki/Subglacial_lake" title="Subglacial lake">
        subglacial lakes
       </a>
       . It was once believed that the lake had been sealed off for 500,000 to one million years but a recent survey suggests that, every so often, there are large flows of water from one lake to another.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Briggs2006_48-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Briggs2006-48">
         [47]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       There is some evidence, in the form of
       <a href="/wiki/Ice_core" title="Ice core">
        ice cores
       </a>
       drilled to about 400 m (1,300 ft) above the water line, that Lake Vostok's waters may contain
       <a href="/wiki/Microorganism" title="Microorganism">
        microbial life
       </a>
       . The frozen surface of the lake shares similarities with
       <a href="/wiki/Jupiter" title="Jupiter">
        Jupiter
       </a>
       's moon,
       <a href="/wiki/Europa_(moon)" title="Europa (moon)">
        Europa
       </a>
       . If life is discovered in Lake Vostok, it would strengthen the argument for the possibility of life on Europa.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49">
        <a href="#cite_note-49">
         [48]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50">
        <a href="#cite_note-50">
         [49]
        </a>
       </sup>
       On 7 February 2008, a NASA team embarked on a mission to
       <a href="/wiki/Lake_Untersee" title="Lake Untersee">
        Lake Untersee
       </a>
       , searching for
       <a href="/wiki/Extremophile" title="Extremophile">
        extremophiles
       </a>
       in its highly alkaline waters. If found, these resilient creatures could further bolster the argument for extraterrestrial life in extremely cold, methane-rich environments.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA-CloroxLake_51-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-NASA-CloroxLake-51">
         [50]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Geology">
        Geology
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:AA_bedrock_bedmap2.4960.tif">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1080" data-file-width="1920" height="124" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/AA_bedrock_bedmap2.4960.tif/lossy-page1-220px-AA_bedrock_bedmap2.4960.tif.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/AA_bedrock_bedmap2.4960.tif/lossy-page1-330px-AA_bedrock_bedmap2.4960.tif.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/AA_bedrock_bedmap2.4960.tif/lossy-page1-440px-AA_bedrock_bedmap2.4960.tif.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:AA_bedrock_bedmap2.4960.tif" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         The bedrock topography of Antarctica, critical to understand dynamic motion of the continental ice sheets
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Geology_of_Antarctica" title="Geology of Antarctica">
        Geology of Antarctica
       </a>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:AntarcticBedrock.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1400" data-file-width="1400" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/AntarcticBedrock.jpg/220px-AntarcticBedrock.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/AntarcticBedrock.jpg/330px-AntarcticBedrock.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/AntarcticBedrock.jpg/440px-AntarcticBedrock.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>

         <div class="magnify">
