          </a>
         </div>
         Subglacial
         <a href="/wiki/Topography" title="Topography">
          topography
         </a>
         and
         <a href="/wiki/Bathymetry" title="Bathymetry">
          bathymetry
         </a>
         of bedrock underlying Antarctica ice sheet
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:AntarcticBedrock2.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1333" data-file-width="1333" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e3/AntarcticBedrock2.jpg/220px-AntarcticBedrock2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e3/AntarcticBedrock2.jpg/330px-AntarcticBedrock2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e3/AntarcticBedrock2.jpg/440px-AntarcticBedrock2.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:AntarcticBedrock2.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         The above map shows the subglacial topography of Antarctica. As indicated by the scale on left-hand side, blue represents portion of Antarctica lying below sea level. The other colours indicate Antarctic bedrock lying above sea level. Each colour represents an interval of 760 m (2,500 ft) in elevation. Map is not corrected for sea level rise or
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Isostatic_rebound" title="Isostatic rebound">
          isostatic rebound
         </a>
         , which would occur if the Antarctic ice sheet completely melted to expose the bedrock surface.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Antarctica_Without_Ice_Sheet.png">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1000" data-file-width="782" height="281" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dc/Antarctica_Without_Ice_Sheet.png/220px-Antarctica_Without_Ice_Sheet.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dc/Antarctica_Without_Ice_Sheet.png/330px-Antarctica_Without_Ice_Sheet.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dc/Antarctica_Without_Ice_Sheet.png/440px-Antarctica_Without_Ice_Sheet.png 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Antarctica_Without_Ice_Sheet.png" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Topographic map of Antarctica after removing the ice sheet and accounting for both isostatic rebound and sea level rise. Hence, this map suggests what Antarctica may have looked like 35 million years ago, when the Earth was warm enough to prevent the formation of large-scale ice sheets in Antarctica.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Geological_history_and_palaeontology">
        Geological history and palaeontology
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       More than 170 million years ago, Antarctica was part of the
       <a href="/wiki/Supercontinent" title="Supercontinent">
        supercontinent
       </a>
       <a href="/wiki/Gondwana" title="Gondwana">
        Gondwana
       </a>
       . Over time, Gondwana gradually broke apart and Antarctica as we know it today was formed around 25 million years ago. Antarctica was not always cold, dry, and covered in ice sheets. At a number of points in its long history, it was farther north, experienced a tropical or temperate climate, was covered in forests, and inhabited by various ancient life forms.
      </p>
      <h4>
       <span id="Palaeozoic_era_.28540.E2.80.93250_Ma.29">
       </span>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Palaeozoic_era_(540–250_Ma)">
        Palaeozoic era (540–250 Ma)
       </span>
      </h4>
      <p>
       During the
       <a href="/wiki/Cambrian" title="Cambrian">
        Cambrian period
       </a>
       , Gondwana had a mild climate. West Antarctica was partially in the
       <a href="/wiki/Northern_Hemisphere" title="Northern Hemisphere">
        Northern Hemisphere
       </a>
       , and during this period large amounts of
       <a href="/wiki/Sandstone" title="Sandstone">
        sandstones
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Limestone" title="Limestone">
        limestones
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Shale" title="Shale">
        shales
       </a>
       were deposited. East Antarctica was at the equator, where sea floor
       <a href="/wiki/Invertebrate" title="Invertebrate">
        invertebrates
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Trilobite" title="Trilobite">
        trilobites
       </a>
       flourished in the tropical seas. By the start of the
       <a href="/wiki/Devonian" title="Devonian">
        Devonian period
       </a>
       (416
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Annum" title="Annum">
        Ma
       </a>
       ), Gondwana was in more southern latitudes and the climate was cooler, though fossils of land plants are known from this time.
       <a href="/wiki/Sand" title="Sand">
        Sand
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Silt" title="Silt">
        silts
       </a>
       were laid down in what is now the
       <a href="/wiki/Ellsworth_Mountains" title="Ellsworth Mountains">
        Ellsworth
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Horlick_Mountains" title="Horlick Mountains">
        Horlick
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Pensacola_Mountains" title="Pensacola Mountains">
        Pensacola Mountains
       </a>
       .
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Glaciation" title="Glaciation">
        Glaciation
       </a>
       began at the end of the Devonian period (360 Ma), as Gondwana became centred on the South Pole and the climate cooled, though
       <a href="/wiki/Antarctic_flora" title="Antarctic flora">
        flora
       </a>
       remained. During the
       <a href="/wiki/Permian" title="Permian">
        Permian
       </a>
       period, the land became dominated by seed plants such as
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Glossopteris" title="Glossopteris">
         Glossopteris
        </a>
       </i>
       , a pteridosperm which grew in swamps. Over time these swamps became deposits of coal in the
       <a href="/wiki/Transantarctic_Mountains" title="Transantarctic Mountains">
        Transantarctic Mountains
       </a>
       . Towards the end of the Permian period, continued warming led to a dry, hot climate over much of Gondwana.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stonehouse_52-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Stonehouse-52">
         [51]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h4>
       <span id="Mesozoic_era_.28250.E2.80.9366_Ma.29">
       </span>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Mesozoic_era_(250–66_Ma)">
        Mesozoic era (250–66 Ma)
       </span>
      </h4>
      <p>
       As a result of continued warming, the polar ice caps melted and much of Gondwana became a desert. In Eastern Antarctica,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Seed_fern" title="Seed fern">
        seed ferns
       </a>
       or pteridosperms became abundant and large amounts of sandstone and shale were laid down at this time.
       <a href="/wiki/Synapsid" title="Synapsid">
        Synapsids
       </a>
       , commonly known as "mammal-like reptiles", were common in Antarctica during the
       <a href="/wiki/Early_Triassic" title="Early Triassic">
        Early Triassic
       </a>
       and included forms such as
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Lystrosaurus" title="Lystrosaurus">
         Lystrosaurus
        </a>
       </i>
       . The Antarctic Peninsula began to form during the
       <a href="/wiki/Jurassic" title="Jurassic">
        Jurassic
       </a>
       period (206–146 Ma), and islands gradually rose out of the ocean.
       <a href="/wiki/Ginkgo" title="Ginkgo">
        Ginkgo
       </a>
       trees, conifers, bennettites, horsetails, ferns and
       <a href="/wiki/Cycad" title="Cycad">
        cycads
       </a>
       were plentiful during this period. In West Antarctica,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Conifer" title="Conifer">
        coniferous
       </a>
       <a href="/wiki/Forest" title="Forest">
        forests
       </a>
       dominated through the entire
       <a href="/wiki/Cretaceous" title="Cretaceous">
        Cretaceous
       </a>
       period (146–66 Ma), though
       <a href="/wiki/Nothofagus" title="Nothofagus">
        southern beech
       </a>
       became more prominent towards the end of this period.
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ammonite" title="Ammonite">
        Ammonites
       </a>
       were common in the seas around Antarctica, and dinosaurs were also present, though only three Antarctic dinosaur
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Genera" title="Genera">
        genera
       </a>
       (
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Cryolophosaurus" title="Cryolophosaurus">
         Cryolophosaurus
        </a>
       </i>
       and
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Glacialisaurus" title="Glacialisaurus">
         Glacialisaurus
        </a>
       </i>
       , from the
       <a href="/wiki/Hanson_Formation" title="Hanson Formation">
        Hanson Formation
       </a>
       ,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SmithPol2007_53-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-SmithPol2007-53">
         [52]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Antarctopelta" title="Antarctopelta">
         Antarctopelta
        </a>
       </i>
       ) have been described to date.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54">
        <a href="#cite_note-54">
         [53]
        </a>
       </sup>
       It was during this era that Gondwana began to break up.
      </p>
      <p>
       However, there is some evidence of antarctic marine glaciation during the
       <a href="/wiki/Cretaceous" title="Cretaceous">
        Cretaceous
       </a>
       period.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55">
        <a href="#cite_note-55">
         [54]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h4>
       <span id="Gondwana_breakup_.28160.E2.80.9323_Ma.29">
       </span>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Gondwana_breakup_(160–23_Ma)">
        Gondwana breakup (160–23 Ma)
       </span>
      </h4>
      <p>
       The cooling of Antarctica occurred stepwise, as the continental spread changed the oceanic currents from longitudinal equator-to-pole temperature-equalising currents to latitudinal currents that preserved and accentuated latitude temperature differences.
      </p>
      <p>
       Africa separated from Antarctica in the Jurassic, around 160 Ma, followed by the
       <a href="/wiki/Indian_subcontinent" title="Indian subcontinent">
        Indian subcontinent
       </a>
       in the early Cretaceous (about 125 Ma). By the end of the Cretaceous, about 66 Ma, Antarctica (then connected to Australia) still had a subtropical climate and flora, complete with a
       <a href="/wiki/Marsupial" title="Marsupial">
        marsupial
       </a>
       <a href="/wiki/Fauna" title="Fauna">
        fauna
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56">
        <a href="#cite_note-56">
         [55]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In the Eocene epoch, about 40 Ma Australia-
       <a href="/wiki/New_Guinea" title="New Guinea">
        New Guinea
       </a>
       separated from Antarctica, so that latitudinal currents could isolate Antarctica from Australia, and the first ice began to appear. During the
       <a href="/wiki/Eocene%E2%80%93Oligocene_extinction_event" title="Eocene–Oligocene extinction event">
        Eocene–Oligocene extinction event
       </a>
       about 34 million years ago, CO
       <sub>
        2
       </sub>
       levels have been found to be about 760 ppm
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57">
        <a href="#cite_note-57">
         [56]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and had been decreasing from earlier levels in the thousands of ppm.
      </p>
      <p>
       Around 23 Ma, the
       <a href="/wiki/Drake_Passage" title="Drake Passage">
        Drake Passage
       </a>
       opened between Antarctica and South America, resulting in the
       <a href="/wiki/Antarctic_Circumpolar_Current" title="Antarctic Circumpolar Current">
        Antarctic Circumpolar Current
       </a>
       that completely isolated the continent. Models of the changes suggest that declining CO
       <sub>
        2
       </sub>
       levels became more important.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DeContoPollard2003_58-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-DeContoPollard2003-58">
         [57]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The ice began to spread, replacing the forests that then covered the continent.
      </p>
      <h4>
       <span id="Neogene_Period_.2823.E2.80.930.05_Ma.29">
       </span>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Neogene_Period_(23–0.05_Ma)">
        Neogene Period (23–0.05 Ma)
       </span>
      </h4>
      <p>
       Since about 15 Ma, the continent has been mostly covered with ice.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Trewby2002_59-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Trewby2002-59">
         [58]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Meyer_Desert_Formation_biota">
        Meyer Desert Formation biota
       </span>
      </h4>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Meyer_Desert_Formation_biota" title="Meyer Desert Formation biota">
        Meyer Desert Formation biota
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       Fossil
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Nothofagus" title="Nothofagus">
         Nothofagus
        </a>
       </i>
       leaves in the Meyer Desert Formation of the
       <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Sirius_Group&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Sirius Group (page does not exist)">
        Sirius Group
       </a>
       show that intermittent warm periods allowed
       <i>
        Nothofagus
       </i>
       shrubs to cling to the
       <a href="/wiki/Dominion_Range" title="Dominion Range">
        Dominion Range
       </a>
       as late as 3–4 Ma (mid-late
       <a href="/wiki/Pliocene" title="Pliocene">
        Pliocene
       </a>
       ).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60">
        <a href="#cite_note-60">
         [59]
        </a>
       </sup>
       After that, the
       <a href="/wiki/Pleistocene" title="Pleistocene">
        Pleistocene
       </a>
       ice age covered the whole continent and destroyed all major plant life on it.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61">
        <a href="#cite_note-61">
         [60]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Present-day">
        Present-day
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Marie_Byrd_Land,_West_Antarctica_by_NASA.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1325" data-file-width="2000" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Marie_Byrd_Land%2C_West_Antarctica_by_NASA.jpg/220px-Marie_Byrd_Land%2C_West_Antarctica_by_NASA.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Marie_Byrd_Land%2C_West_Antarctica_by_NASA.jpg/330px-Marie_Byrd_Land%2C_West_Antarctica_by_NASA.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Marie_Byrd_Land%2C_West_Antarctica_by_NASA.jpg/440px-Marie_Byrd_Land%2C_West_Antarctica_by_NASA.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Marie_Byrd_Land,_West_Antarctica_by_NASA.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Glaciers and rock outcrops in Marie Byrd Land seen from NASA's
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/DC-8" title="DC-8">
          DC-8
         </a>
         aircraft
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       The geological study of Antarctica has been greatly hindered by nearly all of the continent being permanently covered with a thick layer of ice.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62">
        <a href="#cite_note-62">
         [61]
        </a>
       </sup>
       However, new techniques such as
       <a href="/wiki/Remote_sensing" title="Remote sensing">
        remote sensing
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Ground-penetrating_radar" title="Ground-penetrating radar">
        ground-penetrating radar
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Satellite_imagery" title="Satellite imagery">
        satellite imagery
       </a>
       have begun to reveal the structures beneath the ice.
      </p>
      <p>
       Geologically, West Antarctica closely resembles the
       <a href="/wiki/Andes" title="Andes">
        Andes
       </a>
       mountain range of South America.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stonehouse_52-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Stonehouse-52">
         [51]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The
       <a href="/wiki/Antarctic_Peninsula" title="Antarctic Peninsula">
        Antarctic Peninsula
       </a>
       was formed by uplift and
       <a href="/wiki/Metamorphism" title="Metamorphism">
        metamorphism
       </a>
       of sea bed sediments during the late
       <a href="/wiki/Paleozoic" title="Paleozoic">
        Paleozoic
       </a>
       and the early
       <a href="/wiki/Mesozoic" title="Mesozoic">
        Mesozoic
       </a>
       eras. This sediment uplift was accompanied by
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Igneous" title="Igneous">
        igneous
       </a>
       intrusions and
       <a href="/wiki/Volcanism" title="Volcanism">
        volcanism
       </a>
       . The most common rocks in West Antarctica are
       <a href="/wiki/Andesite" title="Andesite">
        andesite
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Rhyolite" title="Rhyolite">
        rhyolite
       </a>
       volcanics formed during the Jurassic period. There is also evidence of volcanic activity, even after the ice sheet had formed, in
       <a href="/wiki/Marie_Byrd_Land" title="Marie Byrd Land">
        Marie Byrd Land
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Alexander_Island" title="Alexander Island">
        Alexander Island
       </a>
       . The only anomalous area of West Antarctica is the
       <a href="/wiki/Ellsworth_Mountains" title="Ellsworth Mountains">
        Ellsworth Mountains
       </a>
       region, where the
       <a href="/wiki/Stratigraphy" title="Stratigraphy">
        stratigraphy
       </a>
       is more similar to East Antarctica.
      </p>
      <p>
       East Antarctica is geologically varied, dating from the
       <a href="/wiki/Precambrian" title="Precambrian">
        Precambrian
       </a>
       era, with some rocks formed more than 3 billion years ago. It is composed of a
       <a href="/wiki/Metamorphic_rock" title="Metamorphic rock">
        metamorphic
       </a>
       and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Igneous" title="Igneous">
        igneous
       </a>
       platform which is the basis of the
       <a href="/wiki/Shield_(geology)" title="Shield (geology)">
        continental shield
       </a>
       . On top of this base are coal and various modern rocks, such as
       <a href="/wiki/Sandstone" title="Sandstone">
        sandstones
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Limestone" title="Limestone">
        limestones
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Shale" title="Shale">
        shales
       </a>
       laid down during the Devonian and Jurassic periods to form the
       <a href="/wiki/Transantarctic_Mountains" title="Transantarctic Mountains">
        Transantarctic Mountains
       </a>
       . In coastal areas such as
       <a href="/wiki/Shackleton_Range" title="Shackleton Range">
        Shackleton Range
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Victoria_Land" title="Victoria Land">
        Victoria Land
       </a>
       some
       <a href="/wiki/Fault_(geology)" title="Fault (geology)">
        faulting
       </a>
       has occurred.
      </p>
      <p>
       The main mineral resource known on the continent is coal.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Trewby2002_59-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Trewby2002-59">
         [58]
        </a>
       </sup>
       It was first recorded near the
       <a href="/wiki/Beardmore_Glacier" title="Beardmore Glacier">
        Beardmore Glacier
       </a>
       by
       <a href="/wiki/Frank_Wild" title="Frank Wild">
        Frank Wild
       </a>
       on the
       <a href="/wiki/Nimrod_Expedition" title="Nimrod Expedition">
        Nimrod Expedition
       </a>
       , and now low-grade coal is known across many parts of the Transantarctic Mountains. The
       <a href="/wiki/Prince_Charles_Mountains" title="Prince Charles Mountains">
        Prince Charles Mountains
       </a>
       contain significant deposits of iron ore. The most valuable resources of Antarctica lie offshore, namely the
       <a href="/wiki/Oil_field" title="Oil field">
        oil
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Natural_gas_field" title="Natural gas field">
        natural gas fields
       </a>
       found in the Ross Sea in 1973. Exploitation of all mineral resources is
       <a href="/wiki/Ban_(law)" title="Ban (law)">
        banned
       </a>
       until 2048 by the
       <a href="/wiki/Protocol_on_Environmental_Protection_to_the_Antarctic_Treaty" title="Protocol on Environmental Protection to the Antarctic Treaty">
        Protocol on Environmental Protection to the Antarctic Treaty
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Climate">
        Climate
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Climate_of_Antarctica" title="Climate of Antarctica">
        Climate of Antarctica
       </a>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Fryxellsee_Opt.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1083" data-file-width="1560" height="153" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/Fryxellsee_Opt.jpg/220px-Fryxellsee_Opt.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/Fryxellsee_Opt.jpg/330px-Fryxellsee_Opt.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/Fryxellsee_Opt.jpg/440px-Fryxellsee_Opt.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Fryxellsee_Opt.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         The
         <a href="/wiki/Blue_ice_(glacial)" title="Blue ice (glacial)">
          blue ice
         </a>
         covering
         <a href="/wiki/Lake_Fryxell" title="Lake Fryxell">
          Lake Fryxell
         </a>
         , in the
         <a href="/wiki/Transantarctic_Mountains" title="Transantarctic Mountains">
          Transantarctic Mountains
         </a>
         , comes from
         <a href="/wiki/Glacier" title="Glacier">
          glacial
         </a>
         meltwater from the
         <a href="/wiki/Canada_Glacier" title="Canada Glacier">
          Canada Glacier
         </a>
         and other smaller glaciers.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:AntarcticaSummer.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1063" data-file-width="1417" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2a/AntarcticaSummer.jpg/220px-AntarcticaSummer.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2a/AntarcticaSummer.jpg/330px-AntarcticaSummer.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2a/AntarcticaSummer.jpg/440px-AntarcticaSummer.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:AntarcticaSummer.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Near the coast, December looks fairly temperate.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       Antarctica is the coldest of
       <a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">
        Earth
       </a>
       's continents. It used to be ice-free until about 34 million years ago, when it became covered with ice.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63">
        <a href="#cite_note-63">
         [62]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The coldest natural air temperature ever recorded on Earth was −89.2 °C (−128.6 °F) at the Soviet (now
       <a href="/wiki/Russia" title="Russia">
        Russian
       </a>
       )
       <a href="/wiki/Vostok_Station" title="Vostok Station">
        Vostok Station
       </a>
       in Antarctica on 21 July 1983.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64">
        <a href="#cite_note-64">
         [63]
        </a>
       </sup>
       For comparison, this is 10.7 °C (20 °F) colder than
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sublimation_(chemistry)" title="Sublimation (chemistry)">
        subliming
       </a>
       <a href="/wiki/Dry_ice" title="Dry ice">
        dry ice
       </a>
       at one atmosphere of partial pressure, but since CO
       <sub>
        2
       </sub>
       only makes up 0.039% of air, temperatures of less than −140 °C (−220 °F)
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AgeeOrton2013_65-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-AgeeOrton2013-65">
         [64]
        </a>
       </sup>
       would be needed to produce dry ice snow in Antarctica. A lower air temperature of −94.7 °C (−138.5 °F) was recorded in 2010 by satellite—however, it may be influenced by ground temperatures and was not recorded at a height of 7 feet above the surface as required for the official air temperature records.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66">
        <a href="#cite_note-66">
         [65]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Antarctica is a frozen desert with little
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Precipitation_(meteorology)" title="Precipitation (meteorology)">
        precipitation
       </a>
       ; the South Pole itself receives less than 10 cm (4 in) per year, on average. Temperatures reach a minimum of between −80 °C (−112 °F) and −89.2 °C (−128.6 °F) in the interior in winter and reach a maximum of between 5 °C (41 °F) and 15 °C (59 °F) near the coast in summer. Sunburn is often a health issue as the snow surface reflects almost all of the ultraviolet light falling on it. Given the latitude, long periods of constant darkness or constant sunlight create climates unfamiliar to human beings in much of the rest of the world.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BAS-weather_67-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-BAS-weather-67">
         [66]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:AntarcticaDomeCSnow.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="688" data-file-width="937" height="162" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/AntarcticaDomeCSnow.jpg/220px-AntarcticaDomeCSnow.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/AntarcticaDomeCSnow.jpg/330px-AntarcticaDomeCSnow.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/AntarcticaDomeCSnow.jpg/440px-AntarcticaDomeCSnow.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:AntarcticaDomeCSnow.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         The snow surface at
         <a href="/wiki/Dome_C" title="Dome C">
          Dome C
         </a>
         <a href="/wiki/Concordia_Station" title="Concordia Station">
          Station
         </a>
         is typical of most of the continent's surface.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       East Antarctica is colder than its western counterpart because of its higher elevation.
       <a href="/wiki/Weather_front" title="Weather front">
        Weather fronts
       </a>
       rarely penetrate far into the continent, leaving the centre cold and dry. Despite the lack of precipitation over the central portion of the continent, ice there lasts for extended periods. Heavy snowfalls are common on the coastal portion of the continent, where snowfalls of up to 1.22 metres (48 in) in 48 hours have been recorded.
      </p>
      <p>
       At the edge of the continent, strong
       <a href="/wiki/Katabatic_wind" title="Katabatic wind">
        katabatic winds
       </a>
       off the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Polar_plateau" title="Polar plateau">
        polar plateau
       </a>
       often blow at storm force. In the interior, wind speeds are typically moderate. During clear days in summer, more
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Solar_radiation" title="Solar radiation">
        solar radiation
       </a>
       reaches the surface at the South Pole than at the
       <a href="/wiki/Equator" title="Equator">
        equator
       </a>
       because of the 24 hours of sunlight each day at the Pole.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CIAfactbook-People_1-3">
        <a href="#cite_note-CIAfactbook-People-1">
         [1]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Antarctica is colder than the
       <a href="/wiki/Arctic" title="Arctic">
        Arctic
       </a>
       for three reasons. First, much of the continent is more than 3,000 m (9,800 ft) above sea level, and temperature decreases with elevation in the
       <a href="/wiki/Troposphere" title="Troposphere">
        troposphere
       </a>
       . Second, the Arctic Ocean covers the north polar zone: the ocean's relative warmth is transferred through the icepack and prevents temperatures in the Arctic regions from reaching the extremes typical of the land surface of Antarctica. Third, the Earth is at
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aphelion" title="Aphelion">
        aphelion
       </a>
       in July (i.e., the Earth is farthest from the Sun in the Antarctic winter), and the Earth is at
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Perihelion" title="Perihelion">
        perihelion
       </a>
       in January (i.e., the Earth is closest to the Sun in the Antarctic summer). The orbital distance contributes to a colder Antarctic winter (and a warmer Antarctic summer) but the first two effects have more impact.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68">
        <a href="#cite_note-68">
         [67]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aurora_australis" title="Aurora australis">
        aurora australis
       </a>
       , commonly known as the southern lights, is a glow observed in the night sky near the South Pole created by the plasma-full
       <a href="/wiki/Solar_wind" title="Solar wind">
        solar winds
       </a>
       that pass by the Earth. Another unique spectacle is
       <a href="/wiki/Diamond_dust" title="Diamond dust">
        diamond dust
       </a>
       , a ground-level cloud composed of tiny ice crystals. It generally forms under otherwise clear or nearly clear skies, so people sometimes also refer to it as clear-sky precipitation. A
       <a href="/wiki/Sun_dog" title="Sun dog">
        sun dog
       </a>
       , a frequent atmospheric
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Optical_phenomenon" title="Optical phenomenon">
        optical phenomenon
       </a>
       , is a bright "spot" beside the true
       <a href="/wiki/Sun" title="Sun">
        sun
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BAS-weather_67-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-BAS-weather-67">
         [66]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Population">
        Population
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       See also:
       <a href="/wiki/Demographics_of_Antarctica" title="Demographics of Antarctica">
        Demographics of Antarctica
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Research_stations_in_Antarctica" title="Research stations in Antarctica">
        Research stations in Antarctica
       </a>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:AmundsenScottSuedpolStation.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1012" data-file-width="1268" height="176" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/90/AmundsenScottSuedpolStation.jpg/220px-AmundsenScottSuedpolStation.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/90/AmundsenScottSuedpolStation.jpg/330px-AmundsenScottSuedpolStation.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/90/AmundsenScottSuedpolStation.jpg/440px-AmundsenScottSuedpolStation.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:AmundsenScottSuedpolStation.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         The "ceremonial"
         <a href="/wiki/South_Pole#Ceremonial_South_Pole" title="South Pole">
          South Pole
         </a>
         , at
         <a href="/wiki/Amundsen%E2%80%93Scott_South_Pole_Station" title="Amundsen–Scott South Pole Station">
          Amundsen–Scott Station
         </a>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       Several governments maintain permanent manned
       <a href="/wiki/Research_station" title="Research station">
        research stations
       </a>
       on the continent. The number of people conducting and supporting scientific research and other work on the continent and its nearby islands varies from about 1,000 in winter to about 5,000 in the summer, giving it a
       <a href="/wiki/Population_density" title="Population density">
        population density
       </a>
       between 70 and 350 inhabitants per million square kilometres (180 and 900 per million square miles) at these times. Many of the stations are staffed year-round, the winter-over personnel typically arriving from their home countries for a one-year assignment. An
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Orthodox_church" title="Orthodox church">
        Orthodox church
       </a>
       —
       <a href="/wiki/Trinity_Church_(Antarctica)" title="Trinity Church (Antarctica)">
        Trinity Church
       </a>
       , opened in 2004 at the Russian
       <a href="/wiki/Bellingshausen_Station" title="Bellingshausen Station">
        Bellingshausen Station
       </a>
       —is manned year-round by one or two priests, who are similarly rotated every year.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69">
        <a href="#cite_note-69">
         [68]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70">
        <a href="#cite_note-70">
         [69]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Port-Lockroy.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1952" data-file-width="2608" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/Port-Lockroy.jpg/220px-Port-Lockroy.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/Port-Lockroy.jpg/330px-Port-Lockroy.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/Port-Lockroy.jpg/440px-Port-Lockroy.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Port-Lockroy.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         <a href="/wiki/Port_Lockroy" title="Port Lockroy">
          Port Lockroy
         </a>
         Museum
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       The first semi-permanent inhabitants of regions near Antarctica (areas situated south of the
       <a href="/wiki/Antarctic_Convergence" title="Antarctic Convergence">
        Antarctic Convergence
       </a>
       ) were British and American
       <a href="/wiki/Seal_hunting" title="Seal hunting">
        sealers
       </a>
       who used to spend a year or more on
       <a href="/wiki/South_Georgia_and_the_South_Sandwich_Islands" title="South Georgia and the South Sandwich Islands">
        South Georgia
       </a>
       , from 1786 onward. During the
       <a href="/wiki/Whaling" title="Whaling">
        whaling
       </a>
       era, which lasted until 1966, the population of that island varied from over 1,000 in the summer (over 2,000 in some years) to some 200 in the winter. Most of the whalers were Norwegian, with an increasing proportion of Britons. The settlements included
       <a href="/wiki/Grytviken" title="Grytviken">
        Grytviken
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Leith_Harbour" title="Leith Harbour">
        Leith Harbour
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/King_Edward_Point" title="King Edward Point">
        King Edward Point
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Stromness,_South_Georgia" title="Stromness, South Georgia">
        Stromness
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Husvik" title="Husvik">
        Husvik
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Prince_Olav_Harbour" title="Prince Olav Harbour">
        Prince Olav Harbour
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Ocean_Harbour" title="Ocean Harbour">
        Ocean Harbour
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Godthul" title="Godthul">
        Godthul
       </a>
       . Managers and other senior officers of the whaling stations often lived together with their families. Among them was the founder of Grytviken, Captain
       <a href="/wiki/Carl_Anton_Larsen" title="Carl Anton Larsen">
        Carl Anton Larsen
       </a>
       , a prominent Norwegian whaler and explorer who, along with his family, adopted British citizenship in 1910.
      </p>
      <p>
       The first child born in the
       <a href="/wiki/Antarctic_Convergence" title="Antarctic Convergence">
        southern polar region
       </a>
       was Norwegian girl
       <a href="/wiki/Solveig_Gunbj%C3%B8rg_Jacobsen" title="Solveig Gunbjørg Jacobsen">
        Solveig Gunbjørg Jacobsen
       </a>
       , born in Grytviken on 8 October 1913, and her birth was registered by the resident British Magistrate of
       <a href="/wiki/South_Georgia_Island" title="South Georgia Island">
        South Georgia
       </a>
       . She was a daughter of Fridthjof Jacobsen, the assistant manager of the whaling station, and Klara Olette Jacobsen. Jacobsen arrived on the island in 1904 and became the manager of Grytviken, serving from 1914 to 1921; two of his children were born on the island.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71">
        <a href="#cite_note-71">
         [70]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Emilio_Marcos_Palma" title="Emilio Marcos Palma">
        Emilio Marcos Palma
       </a>
       was the first person born south of the
       <a href="/wiki/60th_parallel_south" title="60th parallel south">
        60th parallel south
       </a>
       (the continental limit according to the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Antarctic_Treaty" title="Antarctic Treaty">
        Antarctic Treaty
       </a>
       ),
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72">
        <a href="#cite_note-72">
         [71]
        </a>
       </sup>
       as well as the first one born on the Antarctic mainland, in 1978 at
       <a href="/wiki/Esperanza_Base" title="Esperanza Base">
        Base Esperanza
       </a>
       , on the tip of the Antarctic Peninsula;
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73">
        <a href="#cite_note-73">
         [72]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74">
        <a href="#cite_note-74">
         [73]
        </a>
       </sup>
       his parents were sent there along with seven other families by the
       <a href="/wiki/Argentina" title="Argentina">
        Argentine
       </a>
       government to determine if the continent was suitable for family life. In 1984, Juan Pablo Camacho was born at the
       <a href="/wiki/Base_Presidente_Eduardo_Frei_Montalva" title="Base Presidente Eduardo Frei Montalva">
        Frei Montalva Station
       </a>
       , becoming the first Chilean born in Antarctica. Several bases are now home to families with children attending schools at the station.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75">
        <a href="#cite_note-75">
         [74]
        </a>
       </sup>
       As of 2009, eleven children were born in Antarctica (south of the 60th parallel south): eight at the Argentine Esperanza Base
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76">
        <a href="#cite_note-76">
         [75]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and three at the Chilean Frei Montalva Station.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77">
        <a href="#cite_note-77">
         [76]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Biodiversity">
        Biodiversity
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       See also:
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Antarctic_ecozone" title="Antarctic ecozone">
        Antarctic ecozone
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Antarctic_flora" title="Antarctic flora">
        Antarctic flora
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Antarctic_microorganism" title="Antarctic microorganism">
        Antarctic microorganism
       </a>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/Wildlife_of_Antarctica" title="Wildlife of Antarctica">
        Wildlife of Antarctica
       </a>
      </div>
      <div class="center">
       <div class="thumb tnone">

         </a>
         <div class="thumbcaption">

           </a>
          </div>

           <a href="/wiki/Antarctopelta" title="Antarctopelta">
            Antarctopelta
           </a>
          </i>
          fossils
         </div>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Emperor_penguin.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1024" data-file-width="700" height="249" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/Emperor_penguin.jpg/170px-Emperor_penguin.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/Emperor_penguin.jpg/255px-Emperor_penguin.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/Emperor_penguin.jpg/340px-Emperor_penguin.jpg 2x" width="170"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">

          </a>
         </div>
         <a href="/wiki/Emperor_penguin" title="Emperor penguin">
          Emperor penguins
         </a>
         in
         <a href="/wiki/Ross_Sea" title="Ross Sea">
          Ross Sea
         </a>
         , Antarctica
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Animals">
        Animals
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       Few terrestrial
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vertebrates" title="Vertebrates">
        vertebrates
       </a>
       live in Antarctica, and those that do are limited to the sub-Antarctic islands.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78">
        <a href="#cite_note-78">
         [77]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Invertebrate life includes
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Microscopic" title="Microscopic">
        microscopic
       </a>
       <a href="/wiki/Mite" title="Mite">
        mites
       </a>
       like the
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Alaskozetes_antarcticus" title="Alaskozetes antarcticus">
         Alaskozetes antarcticus
        </a>
       </i>
       ,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lice" title="Lice">
        lice
       </a>
       ,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Roundworm" title="Roundworm">
        nematodes
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Tardigrade" title="Tardigrade">
        tardigrades
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Rotifer" title="Rotifer">
        rotifers
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Krill" title="Krill">
        krill
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Springtail" title="Springtail">
        springtails
       </a>
       . The flightless
       <a href="/wiki/Midge" title="Midge">
        midge
       </a>
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Belgica_antarctica" title="Belgica antarctica">
         Belgica antarctica
        </a>
       </i>
       , up to 6 mm (
       <span class="frac nowrap">
        <sup>
         1
        </sup>
        ⁄
        <sub>
         4
        </sub>
       </span>
       in) in size, is the largest purely terrestrial animal in Antarctica.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79">
        <a href="#cite_note-79">
         [78]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The
       <a href="/wiki/Snow_petrel" title="Snow petrel">
        snow petrel
       </a>
       is one of only three birds that breed exclusively in Antarctica.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80">
        <a href="#cite_note-80">
         [79]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Some species of marine animals exist and rely, directly or indirectly, on the phytoplankton. Antarctic sea life includes
       <a href="/wiki/Penguin" title="Penguin">
        penguins
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Blue_whale" title="Blue whale">
        blue whales
       </a>
       ,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Orca" title="Orca">
        orcas
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Colossal_squid" title="Colossal squid">
        colossal squids
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Fur_seal" title="Fur seal">
        fur seals
       </a>
       . The
       <a href="/wiki/Emperor_penguin" title="Emperor penguin">
        emperor penguin
       </a>
       is the only penguin that breeds during the winter in Antarctica, while the
       <a href="/wiki/Ad%C3%A9lie_penguin" title="Adélie penguin">
        Adélie penguin
       </a>
       breeds farther south than any other penguin. The
       <a href="/wiki/Southern_rockhopper_penguin" title="Southern rockhopper penguin">
        southern rockhopper penguin
       </a>
       has distinctive feathers around the eyes, giving the appearance of elaborate eyelashes.
       <a href="/wiki/King_penguin" title="King penguin">
        King penguins
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Chinstrap_penguin" title="Chinstrap penguin">
        chinstrap penguins
       </a>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/Gentoo_penguin" title="Gentoo penguin">
        gentoo penguins
       </a>
       also breed in the Antarctic.
      </p>
      <p>
       The
       <a href="/wiki/Antarctic_fur_seal" title="Antarctic fur seal">
        Antarctic fur seal
       </a>
       was very heavily hunted in the 18th and 19th centuries for its pelt by sealers from the United States and the United Kingdom. The
       <a href="/wiki/Weddell_seal" title="Weddell seal">
        Weddell seal
       </a>
       , a "
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/True_seal" title="True seal">
        true seal
       </a>
       ", is named after
       <a href="/wiki/James_Weddell" title="James Weddell">
        Sir James Weddell
       </a>
       , commander of British sealing expeditions in the
       <a href="/wiki/Weddell_Sea" title="Weddell Sea">
        Weddell Sea
       </a>
       .
       <a href="/wiki/Antarctic_krill" title="Antarctic krill">
        Antarctic krill
       </a>
       , which congregate in large
       <a href="/wiki/Shoaling_and_schooling" title="Shoaling and schooling">
        schools
       </a>
       , is the
       <a href="/wiki/Keystone_species" title="Keystone species">
        keystone species
       </a>
       of the
       <a href="/wiki/Ecosystem" title="Ecosystem">
        ecosystem
       </a>
       of the
       <a href="/wiki/Southern_Ocean" title="Southern Ocean">
        Southern Ocean
       </a>
       , and is an important food organism for whales, seals,
       <a href="/wiki/Leopard_seal" title="Leopard seal">
        leopard seals
       </a>
       , fur seals,
       <a href="/wiki/Squid" title="Squid">
        squid
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Notothenioidei" title="Notothenioidei">
        icefish
       </a>
       , penguins,
       <a href="/wiki/Albatross" title="Albatross">
        albatrosses
       </a>
       and many other birds.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81">
        <a href="#cite_note-81">
         [80]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       A census of sea life carried out during the
       <a href="/wiki/International_Polar_Year" title="International Polar Year">
        International Polar Year
       </a>
       and which involved some 500 researchers was released in 2010. The research is part of the global
       <a href="/wiki/Census_of_Marine_Life" title="Census of Marine Life">
        Census of Marine Life
       </a>
       (CoML) and has disclosed some remarkable findings. More than 235 marine organisms live in both polar regions, having bridged the gap of 12,000 km (7,456 mi). Large animals such as some cetaceans and birds make the round trip annually. More surprising are small forms of life such as
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sea_cucumbers" title="Sea cucumbers">
        sea cucumbers
       </a>
       , and free-swimming snails found in both polar oceans. Various factors may aid in their distribution – fairly uniform temperatures of the deep ocean at the poles and the equator which differ by no more than 5 °C, and the major current systems or marine
       <a href="/wiki/Thermohaline_circulation" title="Thermohaline circulation">
        conveyor belt
       </a>
       which transport eggs and larval stages.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82">
        <a href="#cite_note-82">
         [81]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Fungi">
        Fungi
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Lichen_squamulose.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="589" data-file-width="650" height="199" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/Lichen_squamulose.jpg/220px-Lichen_squamulose.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/Lichen_squamulose.jpg/330px-Lichen_squamulose.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/Lichen_squamulose.jpg/440px-Lichen_squamulose.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Lichen_squamulose.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         About 400 species of
         <a href="/wiki/Lichen" title="Lichen">
          lichen
         </a>
         -forming fungi are known to exist in Antarctica.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       About 1,150 species of fungi have been recorded from Antarctica, of which about 750 are non-lichen-forming and 400 are lichen-forming.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-basplants_83-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-basplants-83">
         [82]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84">
        <a href="#cite_note-84">
         [83]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Some of these species are
       <a href="/wiki/Endolith" title="Endolith">
        cryptoendoliths
       </a>
       as a result of evolution under extreme conditions, and have significantly contributed to shaping the impressive rock formations of the
       <a href="/wiki/McMurdo_Dry_Valleys" title="McMurdo Dry Valleys">
        McMurdo Dry Valleys
       </a>
       and surrounding mountain ridges. The apparently simple morphology, scarcely differentiated structures, metabolic systems and enzymes still active at very low temperatures, and reduced life cycles shown by such fungi make them particularly suited to harsh environments such as the McMurdo Dry Valleys. In particular, their thick-walled and strongly melanised cells make them resistant to
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/UV_light" title="UV light">
        UV light
       </a>
       . Those features can also be observed in algae and
       <a href="/wiki/Cyanobacteria" title="Cyanobacteria">
        cyanobacteria
       </a>
       , suggesting that these are adaptations to the conditions prevailing in Antarctica. This has led to speculation that, if life ever occurred on Mars, it might have looked similar to Antarctic fungi such as
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Cryomyces_antarcticus" title="Cryomyces antarcticus">
         Cryomyces antarcticus
        </a>
       </i>
       , and
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Cryomyces_minteri" title="Cryomyces minteri">
         Cryomyces minteri
        </a>
       </i>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85">
        <a href="#cite_note-85">
         [84]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Some of these fungi are also apparently endemic to Antarctica. Endemic Antarctic fungi also include certain dung-inhabiting species which have had to evolve in response to the double challenge of extreme cold while growing on dung, and the need to survive passage through the gut of warm-blooded animals.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86">
        <a href="#cite_note-86">
         [85]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Plants">
        Plants
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       About 298 million years ago
       <a href="/wiki/Permian" title="Permian">
        Permian
       </a>
       forests started to cover the continent, and
       <a href="/wiki/Tundra" title="Tundra">
        tundra
       </a>
       vegetation survived as late as 15 million years ago,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87">
        <a href="#cite_note-87">
         [86]
        </a>
       </sup>
       but the climate of present-day Antarctica does not allow extensive vegetation to form. A combination of freezing temperatures, poor
       <a href="/wiki/Soil" title="Soil">
        soil
       </a>
       quality, lack of moisture, and lack of sunlight inhibit plant growth. As a result, the diversity of plant life is very low and limited in distribution. The
       <a href="/wiki/Flora" title="Flora">
        flora
       </a>
       of the continent largely consists of
       <a href="/wiki/Bryophyte" title="Bryophyte">
        bryophytes
       </a>
       . There are about 100 species of
       <a href="/wiki/Moss" title="Moss">
        mosses
       </a>
       and 25 species of
       <a href="/wiki/Marchantiophyta" title="Marchantiophyta">
        liverworts
       </a>
       , but only three species of
       <a href="/wiki/Flowering_plant" title="Flowering plant">
        flowering plants
       </a>
       , all of which are found in the Antarctic Peninsula:
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Deschampsia_antarctica" title="Deschampsia antarctica">
         Deschampsia antarctica
        </a>
       </i>
       (Antarctic hair grass),
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Colobanthus_quitensis" title="Colobanthus quitensis">
         Colobanthus quitensis
        </a>
       </i>
       (Antarctic pearlwort) and the non-native
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Poa_annua" title="Poa annua">
         Poa annua
        </a>
       </i>
       (annual bluegrass).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88">
        <a href="#cite_note-88">
         [87]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Growth is restricted to a few weeks in the summer.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-basplants_83-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-basplants-83">
         [82]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-aadplants_89-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-aadplants-89">
         [88]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Other_organisms">
        Other organisms
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Blood_Falls_by_Peter_Rejcek.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1740" data-file-width="2968" height="129" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f5/Blood_Falls_by_Peter_Rejcek.jpg/220px-Blood_Falls_by_Peter_Rejcek.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f5/Blood_Falls_by_Peter_Rejcek.jpg/330px-Blood_Falls_by_Peter_Rejcek.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f5/Blood_Falls_by_Peter_Rejcek.jpg/440px-Blood_Falls_by_Peter_Rejcek.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Blood_Falls_by_Peter_Rejcek.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Red fluid pours out of
         <a href="/wiki/Blood_Falls" title="Blood Falls">
          Blood Falls
         </a>
         at
         <a href="/wiki/Taylor_Glacier" title="Taylor Glacier">
          Taylor Glacier
         </a>
         . The colour derives from
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Iron_oxides" title="Iron oxides">
          iron oxides
         </a>
         .
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       Seven hundred species of algae exist, most of which are
       <a href="/wiki/Phytoplankton" title="Phytoplankton">
        phytoplankton
       </a>
       . Multicoloured
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Snow_algae" title="Snow algae">
        snow algae
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Diatom" title="Diatom">
        diatoms
       </a>
       are especially abundant in the coastal regions during the summer.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-aadplants_89-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-aadplants-89">
         [88]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Bacteria have been found living in the cold and dark as deep as 800 m (0.50 mi; 2,600 ft) under the ice.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT-20130206_90-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-NYT-20130206-90">
         [89]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Conservation">
        Conservation
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Antarctica,_pollution,_environment,_Russia,_Bellingshausen_1.JPG">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="863" data-file-width="1252" height="152" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/Antarctica%2C_pollution%2C_environment%2C_Russia%2C_Bellingshausen_1.JPG/220px-Antarctica%2C_pollution%2C_environment%2C_Russia%2C_Bellingshausen_1.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/Antarctica%2C_pollution%2C_environment%2C_Russia%2C_Bellingshausen_1.JPG/330px-Antarctica%2C_pollution%2C_environment%2C_Russia%2C_Bellingshausen_1.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/Antarctica%2C_pollution%2C_environment%2C_Russia%2C_Bellingshausen_1.JPG/440px-Antarctica%2C_pollution%2C_environment%2C_Russia%2C_Bellingshausen_1.JPG 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Antarctica,_pollution,_environment,_Russia,_Bellingshausen_1.JPG" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Dumping of waste, including old vehicles, such as here at the Russian Bellingshausen Station in 1992, is prohibited since the entry into force of the Protocol on Environmental Protection in 1998.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       The Protocol on Environmental Protection to the Antarctic Treaty (also known as the Environmental Protocol or Madrid Protocol) came into force in 1998, and is the main instrument concerned with conservation and management of
       <a href="/wiki/Biodiversity" title="Biodiversity">
        biodiversity
       </a>
       in Antarctica. The Antarctic Treaty Consultative Meeting is advised on environmental and conservation issues in Antarctica by the Committee for Environmental Protection. A major concern within this committee is the risk to Antarctica from unintentional introduction of non-native species from outside the region.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BridgeHughes73_91-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-BridgeHughes73-91">
         [90]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The passing of the
       <a href="/wiki/Antarctic_Conservation_Act" title="Antarctic Conservation Act">
        Antarctic Conservation Act
       </a>
       (1978) in the U.S. brought several restrictions to U.S. activity on Antarctica. The introduction of alien plants or animals can bring a criminal penalty, as can the extraction of any indigenous species. The
       <a href="/wiki/Overfishing" title="Overfishing">
        overfishing
       </a>
       of
       <a href="/wiki/Krill" title="Krill">
        krill
       </a>
       , which plays a large role in the Antarctic ecosystem, led officials to enact regulations on fishing. The Convention for the Conservation of Antarctic Marine Living Resources (CCAMLR), a treaty that came into force in 1980, requires that regulations managing all Southern Ocean fisheries consider potential effects on the entire Antarctic ecosystem.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CIAfactbook-People_1-4">
        <a href="#cite_note-CIAfactbook-People-1">
         [1]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Despite these new acts, unregulated and illegal fishing, particularly of
       <a href="/wiki/Patagonian_toothfish" title="Patagonian toothfish">
        Patagonian toothfish
       </a>
       (marketed as Chilean Sea Bass in the U.S.), remains a serious problem. The illegal fishing of toothfish has been increasing, with estimates of 32,000
       <a href="/wiki/Tonne" title="Tonne">
        tonnes
       </a>
       (35,300 short tons) in 2000.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-KirbyBBC-2001_92-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-KirbyBBC-2001-92">
         [91]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AUgovAntartica-toothfish_93-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-AUgovAntartica-toothfish-93">
         [92]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Politics">
        Politics
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Emblem_of_the_Antarctic_Treaty.svg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="267" data-file-width="267" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b5/Emblem_of_the_Antarctic_Treaty.svg/220px-Emblem_of_the_Antarctic_Treaty.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b5/Emblem_of_the_Antarctic_Treaty.svg/330px-Emblem_of_the_Antarctic_Treaty.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b5/Emblem_of_the_Antarctic_Treaty.svg/440px-Emblem_of_the_Antarctic_Treaty.svg.png 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Emblem_of_the_Antarctic_Treaty.svg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Emblem of the Antarctic Treaty since 2002.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Antarctica_Station_Map.png">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1250" data-file-width="1500" height="183" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/Antarctica_Station_Map.png/220px-Antarctica_Station_Map.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/Antarctica_Station_Map.png/330px-Antarctica_Station_Map.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/Antarctica_Station_Map.png/440px-Antarctica_Station_Map.png 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Antarctica_Station_Map.png" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         29 national Antarctic programmes together supporting science in Antarctica (2009)
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       Several countries claim sovereignty in certain regions. While a few of these countries have mutually recognised each other's claims,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mutualrecog_94-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-mutualrecog-94">
         [93]
        </a>
       </sup>
       the validity of these claims is not recognised universally.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CIAfactbook-People_1-5">
        <a href="#cite_note-CIAfactbook-People-1">
         [1]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       New claims on Antarctica have been suspended since 1959, although in 2015 Norway formally defined
       <a href="/wiki/Queen_Maud_Land" title="Queen Maud Land">
        Queen Maud Land
       </a>
       as including the unclaimed area between it and the South Pole.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95">
        <a href="#cite_note-95">
         [94]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Antarctica's status is regulated by the 1959
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Antarctic_Treaty" title="Antarctic Treaty">
        Antarctic Treaty
       </a>
       and other related agreements, collectively called the
       <a href="/wiki/Antarctic_Treaty_System" title="Antarctic Treaty System">
        Antarctic Treaty System
       </a>
       . Antarctica is defined as all land and
       <a href="/wiki/Ice_shelf" title="Ice shelf">
        ice shelves
       </a>
       south of 60° S for the purposes of the Treaty System. The treaty was signed by twelve countries including the
       <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">
        Soviet Union
       </a>
       (and later Russia), the United Kingdom,
       <a href="/wiki/Argentina" title="Argentina">
        Argentina
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Chile" title="Chile">
        Chile
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Australia" title="Australia">
        Australia
       </a>
       , and the
       <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">
        United States
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96">
        <a href="#cite_note-96">
         [95]
        </a>
       </sup>
       It set aside Antarctica as a scientific preserve, established freedom of scientific investigation and environmental protection, and banned military activity on Antarctica. This was the first
       <a href="/wiki/Arms_control" title="Arms control">
        arms control
       </a>
       agreement established during the
       <a href="/wiki/Cold_War" title="Cold War">
        Cold War
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       In 1983 the Antarctic Treaty Parties began negotiations on a convention to regulate mining in Antarctica.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97">
        <a href="#cite_note-97">
         [96]
        </a>
       </sup>
       A coalition of international organisations
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98">
        <a href="#cite_note-98">
         [97]
        </a>
       </sup>
       launched a public pressure campaign to prevent any minerals development in the region, led largely by
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Greenpeace_International" title="Greenpeace International">
        Greenpeace International
       </a>
       ,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99">
        <a href="#cite_note-99">
         [98]
        </a>
       </sup>
       which operated its own scientific station—
       <a href="/wiki/World_Park_Base" title="World Park Base">
        World Park Base
       </a>
       —in the Ross Sea region from 1987 until 1991
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100">
        <a href="#cite_note-100">
         [99]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and conducted annual expeditions to document environmental effects of humans on Antarctica.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101">
        <a href="#cite_note-101">
         [100]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In 1988, the Convention on the Regulation of Antarctic Mineral Resources (CRAMRA) was adopted.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102">
        <a href="#cite_note-102">
         [101]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The following year, however, Australia and France announced that they would not ratify the convention, rendering it dead for all intents and purposes. They proposed instead that a comprehensive regime to protect the Antarctic environment be negotiated in its place.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AUgovAntartica-Madrid_103-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-AUgovAntartica-Madrid-103">
         [102]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The Protocol on Environmental Protection to the Antarctic Treaty (the "Madrid Protocol") was negotiated as other countries followed suit and on 14 January 1998 it entered into force.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AUgovAntartica-Madrid_103-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-AUgovAntartica-Madrid-103">
         [102]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104">
        <a href="#cite_note-104">
         [103]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The Madrid Protocol bans all mining in Antarctica, designating Antarctica a "natural reserve devoted to peace and science".
      </p>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:HMS_Endurance,_Portsmouth.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="877" data-file-width="1138" height="170" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/HMS_Endurance%2C_Portsmouth.jpg/220px-HMS_Endurance%2C_Portsmouth.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/HMS_Endurance%2C_Portsmouth.jpg/330px-HMS_Endurance%2C_Portsmouth.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/HMS_Endurance%2C_Portsmouth.jpg/440px-HMS_Endurance%2C_Portsmouth.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:HMS_Endurance,_Portsmouth.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         <a href="/wiki/HMS_Endurance_(A171)" title="HMS Endurance (A171)">
          HMS
          <i>
           Endurance
          </i>
         </a>
         : the
         <a href="/wiki/Royal_Navy" title="Royal Navy">
          Royal Navy
         </a>
         's former
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/British_Antarctica" title="British Antarctica">
          Antarctic
         </a>
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Patrol_ship" title="Patrol ship">
          patrol ship
         </a>
         .
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       The Antarctic Treaty prohibits any
       <a href="/wiki/Military_activity_in_the_Antarctic" title="Military activity in the Antarctic">
        military activity in Antarctica
       </a>
       , including the establishment of military bases and fortifications, military manoeuvres, and weapons testing. Military personnel or equipment are permitted only for scientific research or other peaceful purposes.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105">
        <a href="#cite_note-105">
         [104]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The only documented military land manoeuvre has been the small
       <a href="/wiki/Operaci%C3%B3n_90" title="Operación 90">
        Operation NINETY
       </a>
       by the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Military_of_Argentina" title="Military of Argentina">
        Argentine military
       </a>
       in 1965.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106">
        <a href="#cite_note-106">
         [105]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>

          </a>

        </td>

       </tr>

        </td>

       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>

         <span class="flagicon">

         <span class="flagicon">

          Argentine Antarctica
         </a>
        </td>
        <td>
         25°W to 74°W
        </td>
        <td>
         <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Antarctica,_Argentina_territorial_claim.svg">
          <img alt="Antarctica, Argentina territorial claim.svg" data-file-height="806" data-file-width="806" height="90" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/Antarctica%2C_Argentina_territorial_claim.svg/90px-Antarctica%2C_Argentina_territorial_claim.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/Antarctica%2C_Argentina_territorial_claim.svg/135px-Antarctica%2C_Argentina_territorial_claim.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/Antarctica%2C_Argentina_territorial_claim.svg/180px-Antarctica%2C_Argentina_territorial_claim.svg.png 2x" width="90"/>
         </a>
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
         –
        </td>

         (none)
        </td>
        <td>
         Unclaimed territory
         <br/>
         (
         <a href="/wiki/Marie_Byrd_Land" title="Marie Byrd Land">

         </a>
         )
        </td>

         <br/>
         (except

         </a>
         )
        </td>
        <td>
         <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Antarctica,_unclaimed.svg">
