         </a>
        </td>
       </tr>
      </table>
      <p>
       The Argentine, British and Chilean claims all overlap, and have caused friction. On 18 December 2012, the British
       <a href="/wiki/Foreign_and_Commonwealth_Office" title="Foreign and Commonwealth Office">
        Foreign and Commonwealth Office
       </a>
       named a previously unnamed area
       <a href="/wiki/Queen_Elizabeth_Land" title="Queen Elizabeth Land">
        Queen Elizabeth Land
       </a>
       in tribute to
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Queen_Elizabeth_II" title="Queen Elizabeth II">
        Queen Elizabeth II
       </a>
       's
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Diamond_Jubilee" title="Diamond Jubilee">
        Diamond Jubilee
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Foreign_&amp;_Commonwealth_Office_Press_Release_107-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Foreign_&amp;_Commonwealth_Office_Press_Release-107">
         [106]
        </a>
       </sup>
       On 22 December 2012, the UK ambassador to Argentina, John Freeman, was summoned to the Argentine government as protest against the claim.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BBC_News:_Argentine_Protest_Against_Queen_Elizabeth_Land_108-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-BBC_News:_Argentine_Protest_Against_Queen_Elizabeth_Land-108">
         [107]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Argentine–UK relations had previously been damaged throughout 2012 due to disputes over the sovereignty of the nearby
       <a href="/wiki/Falkland_Islands" title="Falkland Islands">
        Falkland Islands
       </a>
       , and the 30th anniversary of the
       <a href="/wiki/Falklands_War" title="Falklands War">
        Falklands War
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       The areas shown as
       <a href="/wiki/Australian_Antarctic_Territory" title="Australian Antarctic Territory">
        Australia's
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Ross_Dependency" title="Ross Dependency">
        New Zealand's
       </a>
       claims were British territory until they were handed over following the countries' independence. Australia currently claims the largest area. The claims of Britain, Australia, New Zealand, France and Norway are all recognised by each other.
      </p>
      <p>
       Other countries participating as members of the Antarctic Treaty have a territorial interest in Antarctica, but the provisions of the Treaty do not allow them to make their claims while it is in force.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-peecug1_109-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-peecug1-109">
         [108]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-peecug2_110-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-peecug2-110">
         [109]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        <span class="flagicon">
         <img alt="" class="thumbborder" data-file-height="504" data-file-width="720" height="15" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/0/05/Flag_of_Brazil.svg/22px-Flag_of_Brazil.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/0/05/Flag_of_Brazil.svg/33px-Flag_of_Brazil.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/0/05/Flag_of_Brazil.svg/43px-Flag_of_Brazil.svg.png 2x" width="22"/>
        </span>
        <a href="/wiki/Brazil" title="Brazil">
         Brazil
        </a>
        has a designated "
        <a href="/wiki/Brazilian_Antarctica" title="Brazilian Antarctica">
         zone of interest
        </a>
        " that is not an actual claim.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Morris1988_111-0">
         <a href="#cite_note-Morris1988-111">
          [110]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        <span class="flagicon">
         <img alt="" class="thumbborder" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="900" height="15" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cf/Flag_of_Peru.svg/23px-Flag_of_Peru.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cf/Flag_of_Peru.svg/35px-Flag_of_Peru.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cf/Flag_of_Peru.svg/45px-Flag_of_Peru.svg.png 2x" width="23"/>
        </span>
        <a href="/wiki/Peru" title="Peru">
         Peru
        </a>
        has formally reserved its right to make a claim.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-peecug1_109-1">
         <a href="#cite_note-peecug1-109">
          [108]
         </a>
        </sup>
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-peecug2_110-1">
         <a href="#cite_note-peecug2-110">
          [109]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        <span class="flagicon">
         <img alt="" class="thumbborder" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="900" height="15" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/f3/Flag_of_Russia.svg/23px-Flag_of_Russia.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/f3/Flag_of_Russia.svg/35px-Flag_of_Russia.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/f3/Flag_of_Russia.svg/45px-Flag_of_Russia.svg.png 2x" width="23"/>
        </span>
        <a href="/wiki/Russia" title="Russia">
         Russia
        </a>
        has inherited the Soviet Union's right to claim territory under the original Antarctic Treaty.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CIAfactbook-US-RusClaim_112-0">
         <a href="#cite_note-CIAfactbook-US-RusClaim-112">
          [111]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        <span class="flagicon">
         <img alt="" class="thumbborder" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="900" height="15" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/Flag_of_South_Africa.svg/23px-Flag_of_South_Africa.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/Flag_of_South_Africa.svg/35px-Flag_of_South_Africa.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/Flag_of_South_Africa.svg/45px-Flag_of_South_Africa.svg.png 2x" width="23"/>
        </span>
        <a href="/wiki/South_Africa" title="South Africa">
         South Africa
        </a>
        has formally reserved its right to make a claim.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-peecug1_109-2">
         <a href="#cite_note-peecug1-109">
          [108]
         </a>
        </sup>
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-peecug2_110-2">
         <a href="#cite_note-peecug2-110">
          [109]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        <span class="flagicon">
         <img alt="" class="thumbborder" data-file-height="650" data-file-width="1235" height="12" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/a/a4/Flag_of_the_United_States.svg/23px-Flag_of_the_United_States.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/a/a4/Flag_of_the_United_States.svg/35px-Flag_of_the_United_States.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/a/a4/Flag_of_the_United_States.svg/46px-Flag_of_the_United_States.svg.png 2x" width="23"/>
        </span>
        <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">
         United States
        </a>
        reserved its right to make a claim in the original Antarctic Treaty.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CIAfactbook-US-RusClaim_112-1">
         <a href="#cite_note-CIAfactbook-US-RusClaim-112">
          [111]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
      </ul>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Economy">
        Economy
       </span>
      </h2>
      <p>
       There is no economic activity in Antarctica at present, except for fishing off the coast and small-scale
       <a href="/wiki/Tourism_in_Antarctica" title="Tourism in Antarctica">
        tourism
       </a>
       , both based outside Antarctica.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113">
        <a href="#cite_note-113">
         [112]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Although coal,
       <a href="/wiki/Hydrocarbon" title="Hydrocarbon">
        hydrocarbons
       </a>
       , iron ore,
       <a href="/wiki/Platinum" title="Platinum">
        platinum
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Copper" title="Copper">
        copper
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Chromium" title="Chromium">
        chromium
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Nickel" title="Nickel">
        nickel
       </a>
       , gold and other minerals have been found, they have not been in large enough quantities to exploit.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114">
        <a href="#cite_note-114">
         [113]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The 1991
       <a href="/wiki/Protocol_on_Environmental_Protection_to_the_Antarctic_Treaty" title="Protocol on Environmental Protection to the Antarctic Treaty">
        Protocol on Environmental Protection to the Antarctic Treaty
       </a>
       also restricts a struggle for resources. In 1998, a compromise agreement was reached to place an indefinite ban on mining, to be reviewed in 2048, further limiting economic development and exploitation. The primary economic activity is the capture and offshore trading of fish. Antarctic fisheries in 2000–01 reported landing 112,934 tonnes.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115">
        <a href="#cite_note-115">
         [114]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Antarctic-Postal-Services.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1952" data-file-width="2608" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/Antarctic-Postal-Services.jpg/220px-Antarctic-Postal-Services.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/Antarctic-Postal-Services.jpg/330px-Antarctic-Postal-Services.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/Antarctic-Postal-Services.jpg/440px-Antarctic-Postal-Services.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Antarctic-Postal-Services.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Post office
         <a href="/wiki/Camp_Academia#Tangra_1091_Post_Office" title="Camp Academia">
          Tangra 1091
         </a>
         Antarctic postal services of the Bulgarian scientific station
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       Small-scale "expedition tourism" has existed since 1957 and is currently subject to Antarctic Treaty and Environmental Protocol provisions, but in effect self-regulated by the
       <a href="/wiki/International_Association_of_Antarctica_Tour_Operators" title="International Association of Antarctica Tour Operators">
        International Association of Antarctica Tour Operators
       </a>
       (IAATO). Not all vessels associated with Antarctic tourism are members of IAATO, but IAATO members account for 95% of the tourist activity. Travel is largely by small or medium ship, focusing on specific scenic locations with accessible concentrations of iconic wildlife. A total of 37,506 tourists visited during the 2006–07
       <a href="/wiki/Southern_Hemisphere" title="Southern Hemisphere">
        Austral summer
       </a>
       with nearly all of them coming from commercial ships; 38,478 were recorded in 2015–16.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116">
        <a href="#cite_note-116">
         [115]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117">
        <a href="#cite_note-117">
         [116]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118">
        <a href="#cite_note-118">
         [117]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       There has been some concern over the potential adverse environmental and ecosystem effects caused by the influx of visitors. Some environmentalists and scientists have made a call for stricter regulations for ships and a tourism quota.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119">
        <a href="#cite_note-119">
         [118]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The primary response by Antarctic Treaty Parties has been to develop, through their Committee for Environmental Protection and in partnership with IAATO, "site use guidelines" setting landing limits and closed or restricted zones on the more frequently visited sites. Antarctic sightseeing flights (which did not land) operated out of Australia and New Zealand until the fatal crash of
       <a href="/wiki/Air_New_Zealand_Flight_901" title="Air New Zealand Flight 901">
        Air New Zealand Flight 901
       </a>
       in 1979 on Mount Erebus, which killed all 257 aboard.
       <a href="/wiki/Qantas" title="Qantas">
        Qantas
       </a>
       resumed commercial overflights to Antarctica from Australia in the mid-1990s.
      </p>
      <p>
       Antarctic fisheries in 1998–99 (1 July – 30 June) reported landing 119,898 tonnes legally.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120">
        <a href="#cite_note-120">
         [119]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       About thirty countries maintain about seventy
       <a href="/wiki/Research_stations_in_Antarctica" title="Research stations in Antarctica">
        research stations
       </a>
       (40 year-round or permanent, and 30 summer-only) in Antarctica, with an approximate population of 4000 in summer and 1000 in winter.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CIAfactbook-People_1-6">
        <a href="#cite_note-CIAfactbook-People-1">
         [1]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The
       <a href="/wiki/ISO_3166-1_alpha-2" title="ISO 3166-1 alpha-2">
        ISO 3166-1 alpha-2
       </a>
       "AQ" is assigned to the entire continent regardless of jurisdiction. Different
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Country_calling_code" title="Country calling code">
        country calling codes
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Currency" title="Currency">
        currencies
       </a>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121">
        <a href="#cite_note-121">
         [120]
        </a>
       </sup>
       are used for different settlements, depending on the administrating country. The
       <a href="/wiki/Antarctican_dollar" title="Antarctican dollar">
        Antarctican dollar
       </a>
       , a souvenir item sold in the United States and Canada, is not legal tender.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CIAfactbook-People_1-7">
        <a href="#cite_note-CIAfactbook-People-1">
         [1]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Symes_122-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Symes-122">
         [121]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Research">
        Research
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">

       <a href="/wiki/Research_stations_in_Antarctica" title="Research stations in Antarctica">

       </a>
      </div>

        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Amundsen-Scott_marsstation_ray_h_edit.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         A
         <a href="/wiki/Full_moon" title="Full moon">
          full moon
         </a>
         and 25-second exposure allowed sufficient light for this photo to be taken at
         <a href="/wiki/Amundsen%E2%80%93Scott_South_Pole_Station" title="Amundsen–Scott South Pole Station">
          Amundsen–Scott South Pole Station
         </a>
         during the long Antarctic night. The station can be seen at far left, the
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Power_plant" title="Power plant">
          power plant
         </a>
         in the centre and the mechanic's garage in the lower right. The green light in the background is the
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aurora_Australis" title="Aurora Australis">
          aurora.
         </a>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       Each year, scientists from 28 different nations conduct
       <a href="/wiki/Experiment" title="Experiment">
        experiments
       </a>
       not reproducible in any other place in the world. In the summer more than 4,000 scientists operate
       <a href="/wiki/Research_station" title="Research station">
        research stations
       </a>
       ; this number decreases to just over 1,000 in the winter.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CIAfactbook-People_1-8">
        <a href="#cite_note-CIAfactbook-People-1">
         [1]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <a href="/wiki/McMurdo_Station" title="McMurdo Station">
        McMurdo Station
       </a>
       , which is the largest research station in Antarctica, is capable of housing more than 1,000 scientists, visitors, and tourists.
      </p>
      <p>
       Researchers include
       <a href="/wiki/Biology" title="Biology">
        biologists
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Geology" title="Geology">
        geologists
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Oceanography" title="Oceanography">
        oceanographers
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Physics" title="Physics">
        physicists
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Astronomy" title="Astronomy">
        astronomers
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Glaciology" title="Glaciology">
        glaciologists
       </a>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/Meteorology" title="Meteorology">
        meteorologists
       </a>
       . Geologists tend to study
       <a href="/wiki/Plate_tectonics" title="Plate tectonics">
        plate tectonics
       </a>
       , meteorites from
       <a href="/wiki/Outer_space" title="Outer space">
        outer space
       </a>
       , and resources from the breakup of the supercontinent
       <a href="/wiki/Gondwana" title="Gondwana">
        Gondwana
       </a>
       . Glaciologists in Antarctica are concerned with the study of the history and
       <a href="/wiki/Ice-sheet_dynamics" title="Ice-sheet dynamics">
        dynamics
       </a>
       of floating ice,
       <a href="/wiki/Snow" title="Snow">
        seasonal snow
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Glacier" title="Glacier">
        glaciers
       </a>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/Ice_sheet" title="Ice sheet">
        ice sheets
       </a>
       . Biologists, in addition to examining the wildlife, are interested in how harsh temperatures and the presence of people affect adaptation and survival strategies in a wide variety of organisms. Medical physicians have made discoveries concerning the spreading of viruses and the body's response to extreme seasonal temperatures. Astrophysicists at
       <a href="/wiki/Amundsen%E2%80%93Scott_South_Pole_Station" title="Amundsen–Scott South Pole Station">
        Amundsen–Scott South Pole Station
       </a>
       study the celestial dome and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cosmic_microwave_background_radiation" title="Cosmic microwave background radiation">
        cosmic microwave background radiation
       </a>
       . Many astronomical observations are better made from the interior of Antarctica than from most surface locations because of the high elevation, which results in a thin atmosphere; low temperature, which minimises the amount of water vapour in the atmosphere; and absence of
       <a href="/wiki/Light_pollution" title="Light pollution">
        light pollution
       </a>
       , thus allowing for a view of space clearer than anywhere else on Earth. Antarctic ice serves as both the shield and the detection medium for the largest
       <a href="/wiki/IceCube_Neutrino_Observatory" title="IceCube Neutrino Observatory">
        neutrino telescope
       </a>
       in the world, built 2 km (1.2 mi) below Amundsen–Scott station.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123">
        <a href="#cite_note-123">
         [122]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Since the 1970s an important focus of study has been the
       <a href="/wiki/Ozone_layer" title="Ozone layer">
        ozone layer
       </a>
       in the
       <a href="/wiki/Atmosphere" title="Atmosphere">
        atmosphere
       </a>
       above Antarctica. In 1985, three British scientists working on data they had gathered at
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Halley_Station" title="Halley Station">
        Halley Station
       </a>
       on the
       <a href="/wiki/Brunt_Ice_Shelf" title="Brunt Ice Shelf">
        Brunt Ice Shelf
       </a>
       discovered the existence of a hole in this layer. It was eventually determined that the destruction of the ozone was caused by
       <a href="/wiki/Chlorofluorocarbon" title="Chlorofluorocarbon">
        chlorofluorocarbons
       </a>
       (CFCs) emitted by human products. With the ban of CFCs in the
       <a href="/wiki/Montreal_Protocol" title="Montreal Protocol">
        Montreal Protocol
       </a>
       of 1989, climate projections indicate that the ozone layer will return to 1980 levels between 2050 and 2070.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-adelie_124-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-adelie-124">
         [123]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       In September 2006
       <a href="/wiki/NASA" title="NASA">
        NASA
       </a>
       satellite data revealed that the Antarctic
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ozone_hole" title="Ozone hole">
        ozone hole
       </a>
       was larger than at any other time on record, at 2,750,000 km
       <sup>
        2
       </sup>
       (1,060,000 sq mi).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ozone_record_125-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-ozone_record-125">
         [124]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The impacts of the depleted ozone layer on climate changes occurring in Antarctica are not well understood.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-adelie_124-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-adelie-124">
         [123]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       In 2007
       <a href="/wiki/The_Polar_Geospatial_Center" title="The Polar Geospatial Center">
        The Polar Geospatial Center
       </a>
       was founded. The Polar Geospatial Center uses
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Geospatial_technology" title="Geospatial technology">
        geospatial
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Remote_sensing" title="Remote sensing">
        remote sensing
       </a>
       technology to provide mapping services to American federally funded research teams. Currently, the Polar Geospatial Center can image all of Antarctica at 50 cm resolution every 45 days.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126">
        <a href="#cite_note-126">
         [125]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       On 6 September 2007
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Belgian" title="Belgian">
        Belgian
       </a>
       -based International Polar Foundation unveiled the
       <a href="/wiki/Princess_Elisabeth_Antarctica" title="Princess Elisabeth Antarctica">
        Princess Elisabeth station
       </a>
       , the world's first zero-emissions polar science station in Antarctica to
       <a href="/wiki/Research" title="Research">
        research
       </a>
       <a href="/wiki/Climate_change" title="Climate change">
        climate change
       </a>
       . Costing $16.3 million, the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Prefabricated" title="Prefabricated">
        prefabricated
       </a>
       station, which is part of the
       <a href="/wiki/International_Polar_Year" title="International Polar Year">
        International Polar Year
       </a>
       , was shipped to the South Pole from
       <a href="/wiki/Belgium" title="Belgium">
        Belgium
       </a>
       by the end of 2008 to monitor the
       <a href="/wiki/Health" title="Health">
        health
       </a>
       of the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Polar_region" title="Polar region">
        polar regions
       </a>
       . Belgian polar
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Explorer" title="Explorer">
        explorer
       </a>
       <a href="/wiki/Alain_Hubert" title="Alain Hubert">
        Alain Hubert
       </a>
       stated: "This base will be the first of its kind to produce zero emissions, making it a unique model of how energy should be used in the Antarctic." Johan Berte is the leader of the station design team and manager of the project which conducts research in
       <a href="/wiki/Climatology" title="Climatology">
        climatology
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Glaciology" title="Glaciology">
        glaciology
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Microbiology" title="Microbiology">
        microbiology
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127">
        <a href="#cite_note-127">
         [126]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       In January 2008
       <a href="/wiki/British_Antarctic_Survey" title="British Antarctic Survey">
        British Antarctic Survey
       </a>
       (BAS) scientists, led by Hugh Corr and David Vaughan, reported (in the journal
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Nature_Geoscience" title="Nature Geoscience">
         Nature Geoscience
        </a>
       </i>
       ) that 2,200 years ago, a
       <a href="/wiki/Volcano" title="Volcano">
        volcano
       </a>
       erupted under Antarctica's ice sheet (based on
       <a href="/wiki/Aerial_survey" title="Aerial survey">
        airborne survey
       </a>
       with radar images). The biggest eruption in Antarctica in the last 10,000 years, the volcanic ash was found deposited on the ice surface under the
       <a href="/wiki/Hudson_Mountains" title="Hudson Mountains">
        Hudson Mountains
       </a>
       , close to
       <a href="/wiki/Pine_Island_Glacier" title="Pine Island Glacier">
        Pine Island Glacier
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128">
        <a href="#cite_note-128">
         [127]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       A study from 2014 estimated that during the
       <a href="/wiki/Pleistocene" title="Pleistocene">
        Pleistocene
       </a>
       , the
       <a href="/wiki/East_Antarctic_Ice_Sheet" title="East Antarctic Ice Sheet">
        East Antarctic Ice Sheet
       </a>
       (EAIS) thinned by at least 500 m (1,600 ft), and that thinning since the
       <a href="/wiki/Last_Glacial_Maximum" title="Last Glacial Maximum">
        Last Glacial Maximum
       </a>
       for the EAIS area is less than 50 m (160 ft) and probably started after c. 14 ka.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129">
        <a href="#cite_note-129">
         [128]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Meteorites">
        Meteorites
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:ALH84001.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="282" data-file-width="442" height="140" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/ALH84001.jpg/220px-ALH84001.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/ALH84001.jpg/330px-ALH84001.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/ALH84001.jpg/440px-ALH84001.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:ALH84001.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Antarctic meteorite, named
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ALH84001" title="ALH84001">
          ALH84001
         </a>
         , from
         <a href="/wiki/Mars" title="Mars">
          Mars
         </a>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       <a href="/wiki/Meteorite" title="Meteorite">
        Meteorites
       </a>
       from Antarctica are an important area of study of material formed early in the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Solar_system" title="Solar system">
        solar system
       </a>
       ; most are thought to come from
       <a href="/wiki/Asteroid" title="Asteroid">
        asteroids
       </a>
       , but some may have originated on larger
       <a href="/wiki/Planet" title="Planet">
        planets
       </a>
       . The first meteorite was found in 1912, and named the
       <a href="/wiki/Adelie_Land_meteorite" title="Adelie Land meteorite">
        Adelie Land meteorite
       </a>
       . In 1969, a Japanese expedition discovered nine meteorites. Most of these meteorites have fallen onto the ice sheet in the last million years. Motion of the ice sheet tends to concentrate the meteorites at blocking locations such as mountain ranges, with wind erosion bringing them to the surface after centuries beneath accumulated snowfall. Compared with meteorites collected in more temperate regions on Earth, the Antarctic meteorites are well-preserved.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-meteorite_130-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-meteorite-130">
         [129]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       This large collection of meteorites allows a better understanding of the abundance of meteorite types in the solar system and how meteorites relate to asteroids and comets. New types of meteorites and rare meteorites have been found. Among these are pieces blasted off the Moon, and probably Mars, by impacts. These specimens, particularly
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ALH84001" title="ALH84001">
        ALH84001
       </a>
       discovered by
       <a href="/wiki/ANSMET" title="ANSMET">
        ANSMET
       </a>
       , are at the centre of the controversy about possible evidence of microbial life on Mars. Because meteorites in space absorb and record cosmic radiation, the time elapsed since the meteorite hit the Earth can be determined from laboratory studies. The elapsed time since fall, or terrestrial residence age, of a meteorite represents more information that might be useful in environmental studies of Antarctic ice sheets.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-meteorite_130-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-meteorite-130">
         [129]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       In 2006 a team of researchers from
       <a href="/wiki/Ohio_State_University" title="Ohio State University">
        Ohio State University
       </a>
       used gravity measurements by NASA's
       <a href="/wiki/Gravity_Recovery_and_Climate_Experiment" title="Gravity Recovery and Climate Experiment">
        GRACE
       </a>
       satellites to discover the 500-kilometre-wide (300 mi)
       <a href="/wiki/Wilkes_Land_crater" title="Wilkes Land crater">
        Wilkes Land crater
       </a>
       , which probably formed about 250 million years ago.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-crater_131-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-crater-131">
         [130]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       In January 2013 an 18 kg (40 lb) meteorite was discovered frozen in ice on the Nansen ice field by a Search for Antarctic Meteorites, Belgian Approach (SAMBA) mission.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132">
        <a href="#cite_note-132">
         [131]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       In January 2015 reports emerged of a 2-kilometre (1.2 mi)
       <a href="/wiki/King_Baudouin_Ice_Shelf#Supposed_meteor_crater" title="King Baudouin Ice Shelf">
        circular structure
       </a>
       , supposedly a meteorite crater, on the surface snow of
       <a href="/wiki/King_Baudouin_Ice_Shelf" title="King Baudouin Ice Shelf">
        King Baudouin Ice Shelf
       </a>
       . Satellite images from 25 years ago seemingly show it.
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Ice_mass_and_global_sea_level">
        Ice mass and global sea level
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       See also:
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Current_sea_level_rise" title="Current sea level rise">
        Current sea level rise
       </a>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;">
        <div class="PopUpMediaTransform" id="mwe_player_0" style="width:300px;" videopayload='&lt;div class="mediaContainer" style="width:512px"&gt;&lt;video id="mwe_player_1" poster="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/90/Flow_of_Ice_Across_Antarctica.ogv/512px--Flow_of_Ice_Across_Antarctica.ogv.jpg" controls="" preload="none" autoplay="" style="width:512px;height:288px" class="kskin" data-durationhint="83.066666666667" data-startoffset="0" data-mwtitle="Flow_of_Ice_Across_Antarctica.ogv" data-mwprovider="wikimediacommons"&gt;&lt;source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/9/90/Flow_of_Ice_Across_Antarctica.ogv" type="video/ogg; codecs=&amp;quot;theora, vorbis&amp;quot;" data-title="Original Ogg file, 512 × 288 (1.76 Mbps)" data-shorttitle="Ogg source" data-width="512" data-height="288" data-bandwidth="1761038" data-framerate="30"/&gt;&lt;source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/9/90/Flow_of_Ice_Across_Antarctica.ogv/Flow_of_Ice_Across_Antarctica.ogv.160p.webm" type="video/webm; codecs=&amp;quot;vp8, vorbis&amp;quot;" data-title="Low bandwidth WebM (160P)" data-shorttitle="WebM 160P" data-transcodekey="160p.webm" data-width="284" data-height="160" data-bandwidth="90568" data-framerate="30"/&gt;&lt;source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/9/90/Flow_of_Ice_Across_Antarctica.ogv/Flow_of_Ice_Across_Antarctica.ogv.240p.webm" type="video/webm; codecs=&amp;quot;vp8, vorbis&amp;quot;" data-title="Small WebM (240P)" data-shorttitle="WebM 240P" data-transcodekey="240p.webm" data-width="426" data-height="240" data-bandwidth="177208" data-framerate="30"/&gt;&lt;/video&gt;&lt;/div&gt;'>
         <img alt="File:Flow of Ice Across Antarctica.ogv" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/90/Flow_of_Ice_Across_Antarctica.ogv/300px--Flow_of_Ice_Across_Antarctica.ogv.jpg" style="width:300px;height:169px"/>
         <a href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/9/90/Flow_of_Ice_Across_Antarctica.ogv" target="new" title="Play media">
          <span class="play-btn-large">
           <span class="mw-tmh-playtext">
            Play media
           </span>
          </span>
         </a>
        </div>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Flow_of_Ice_Across_Antarctica.ogv" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         The motion of ice in Antarctica
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       Due to its location at the South Pole, Antarctica receives relatively little solar radiation. This means that it is a very cold continent where water is mostly in the form of ice.
       <a href="/wiki/Precipitation" title="Precipitation">
        Precipitation
       </a>
       is low (most of Antarctica is a
       <a href="/wiki/Desert" title="Desert">
        desert
       </a>
       ) and almost always in the form of snow, which accumulates and forms a giant ice sheet which covers the land. Parts of this ice sheet form moving glaciers known as
       <a href="/wiki/Ice_stream" title="Ice stream">
        ice streams
       </a>
       , which flow towards the edges of the continent. Next to the continental shore are many
       <a href="/wiki/Ice_shelf" title="Ice shelf">
        ice shelves
       </a>
       . These are floating extensions of outflowing glaciers from the continental ice mass. Offshore, temperatures are also low enough that ice is formed from
       <a href="/wiki/Seawater" title="Seawater">
        seawater
       </a>
       through most of the year. It is important to understand the various types of Antarctic ice to understand possible effects on sea levels and the implications of global cooling.
      </p>
      <p>
       Sea ice extent expands annually in the Antarctic winter and most of this ice melts in the summer. This ice is formed from the ocean water and floats in the same water and thus does not contribute to rise in sea level. The
       <a href="/wiki/Measurement_of_sea_ice#Sea_ice_extent" title="Measurement of sea ice">
        extent
       </a>
       of
       <a href="/wiki/Sea_ice" title="Sea ice">
        sea ice
       </a>
       around Antarctica has remained roughly constant in recent decades, although the thickness changes are unclear.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-United_Nations_Environment_Programme_133-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-United_Nations_Environment_Programme-133">
         [132]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-National_Snow_and_Ice_Data_Center_134-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-National_Snow_and_Ice_Data_Center-134">
         [133]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Melting of floating ice shelves (ice that originated on the land) does not in itself contribute much to sea-level rise (since the ice displaces only its own mass of water). However it is the outflow of the ice from the land to form the ice shelf which causes a rise in global sea level. This effect is offset by snow falling back onto the continent. Recent decades have witnessed several dramatic collapses of large ice shelves around the coast of Antarctica, especially along the Antarctic Peninsula. Concerns have been raised that disruption of ice shelves may result in increased glacial outflow from the continental ice mass.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RignotCasassa2004_135-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-RignotCasassa2004-135">
         [134]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       On the continent itself, the large volume of ice present stores around 70% of the world's fresh water.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-howstuffworks_45-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-howstuffworks-45">
         [44]
        </a>
       </sup>
       This ice sheet is constantly gaining ice from snowfall and losing ice through outflow to the sea. Overall, the net change is slightly positive at approximately 82
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gigatonnes" title="Gigatonnes">
        gigatonnes
       </a>
       (Gt) per year (with significant regional variation), reducing global sea-level rise by 0.23 mm per year.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ZwallyEtAl2015_136-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-ZwallyEtAl2015-136">
         [135]
        </a>
       </sup>
       However, NASA's Climate Change website indicates an overall trend of greater than 100 gigatonnes of ice loss per year since 2002.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-landice_137-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-landice-137">
         [136]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       East Antarctica is a cold region with a ground base
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Height_above_mean_sea_level" title="Height above mean sea level">
        above sea level
       </a>
       and occupies most of the continent. This area is dominated by small accumulations of snowfall which becomes ice and thus eventually seaward glacial flows. The mass balance of the
       <a href="/wiki/East_Antarctic_Ice_Sheet" title="East Antarctic Ice Sheet">
        East Antarctic Ice Sheet
       </a>
       as a whole is thought to be slightly positive (lowering sea level) or near to balance.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ShepherdWingham2007_138-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-ShepherdWingham2007-138">
         [137]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RignotBamber2008_139-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-RignotBamber2008-139">
         [138]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Shepherdetal2012_140-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Shepherdetal2012-140">
         [139]
        </a>
       </sup>
       However, increased ice outflow has been suggested in some regions.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RignotBamber2008_139-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-RignotBamber2008-139">
         [138]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ChenWilson2008_141-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-ChenWilson2008-141">
         [140]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Effects_of_global_warming">
        Effects of global warming
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="thumb tmulti tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:234px;max-width:234px">
        <div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:232px;max-width:232px">
         <div class="thumbimage">
          <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:AntarcticaTemps_1957-2006.jpg">
           <img alt="Antarctican Temperature" data-file-height="480" data-file-width="720" height="153" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/AntarcticaTemps_1957-2006.jpg/230px-AntarcticaTemps_1957-2006.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/AntarcticaTemps_1957-2006.jpg/345px-AntarcticaTemps_1957-2006.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/AntarcticaTemps_1957-2006.jpg/460px-AntarcticaTemps_1957-2006.jpg 2x" width="230"/>
          </a>
         </div>
         <div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">
          <center>
           Warming trend from 1957 to 2006
          </center>
         </div>
        </div>
        <div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:232px;max-width:232px">
         <div class="thumbimage">
          <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:AntarcticaTemps_1957-2006a.jpg">
           <img alt="Legend" data-file-height="50" data-file-width="300" height="38" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/AntarcticaTemps_1957-2006a.jpg/230px-AntarcticaTemps_1957-2006a.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/1/1a/AntarcticaTemps_1957-2006a.jpg 1.5x" width="230"/>
          </a>
         </div>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       See also:
       <a href="/wiki/Global_warming_in_Antarctica" title="Global warming in Antarctica">
        Global warming in Antarctica
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Antarctic_sea_ice" title="Antarctic sea ice">
        Antarctic sea ice
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       Some of Antarctica has been warming up; particularly strong warming has been noted on the Antarctic Peninsula. A study by Eric Steig published in 2009 noted for the first time that the continent-wide average surface temperature trend of Antarctica is slightly positive at &gt;0.05 °C (0.09 °F) per decade from 1957 to 2006. This study also noted that West Antarctica has warmed by more than 0.1 °C (0.2 °F) per decade in the last 50 years, and this warming is strongest in winter and spring. This is partly offset by autumn cooling in East Antarctica.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SteigSchneider2009_142-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-SteigSchneider2009-142">
         [141]
        </a>
       </sup>
       There is evidence from one study that Antarctica is
       <a href="/wiki/Global_warming" title="Global warming">
        warming
       </a>
       as a result of human
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide_emissions" title="Carbon dioxide emissions">
        carbon dioxide emissions
       </a>
       ,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gillett2008_143-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Gillett2008-143">
         [142]
        </a>
       </sup>
       but this remains ambiguous.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Steig2013_144-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Steig2013-144">
         [143]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The amount of surface warming in West Antarctica, while large, has not led to appreciable melting at the surface, and is not directly affecting the
       <a href="/wiki/West_Antarctic_Ice_Sheet" title="West Antarctic Ice Sheet">
        West Antarctic Ice Sheet
       </a>
       's contribution to sea level. Instead the recent increases in glacier outflow are believed to be due to an inflow of warm water from the deep ocean, just off the
       <a href="/wiki/Continental_shelf" title="Continental shelf">
        continental shelf
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PayneVieli2004_145-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-PayneVieli2004-145">
         [144]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ThomaJenkins2008_146-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-ThomaJenkins2008-146">
         [145]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The net contribution to sea level from the Antarctic Peninsula is more likely to be a direct result of the much greater atmospheric warming there.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-147">
        <a href="#cite_note-147">
         [146]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       In 2002 the Antarctic Peninsula's
       <a href="/wiki/Larsen_Ice_Shelf#Larsen_A,_B_&amp;_C_sectors" title="Larsen Ice Shelf">
        Larsen-B
       </a>
       ice shelf collapsed.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-148">
        <a href="#cite_note-148">
         [147]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Between 28 February and 8 March 2008, about 570 km
       <sup>
        2
       </sup>
       (220 sq mi) of ice from the
       <a href="/wiki/Wilkins_Sound" title="Wilkins Sound">
        Wilkins Ice Shelf
       </a>
       on the southwest part of the peninsula collapsed, putting the remaining 15,000 km
       <sup>
        2
       </sup>
       (5,800 sq mi) of the ice shelf at risk. The ice was being held back by a "thread" of ice about 6 km (4 mi) wide,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-149">
        <a href="#cite_note-149">
         [148]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-150">
        <a href="#cite_note-150">
         [149]
        </a>
       </sup>
       prior to its collapse on 5 April 2009.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-151">
        <a href="#cite_note-151">
         [150]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-152">
        <a href="#cite_note-152">
         [151]
        </a>
       </sup>
       According to
       <a href="/wiki/NASA" title="NASA">
        NASA
       </a>
       , the most widespread Antarctic surface melting of the past 30 years occurred in 2005, when an area of ice comparable in size to California briefly melted and refroze; this may have resulted from temperatures rising to as high as 5 °C (41 °F).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-153">
        <a href="#cite_note-153">
         [152]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       A study published in
       <i>
        Nature Geoscience
       </i>
       in 2013 (online in December 2012) identified central West Antarctica as one of the fastest-warming regions on Earth. The researchers present a complete temperature record from Antarctica's Byrd Station and assert that it "reveals a linear increase in annual temperature between 1958 and 2010 by 2.4±1.2 °C".
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-154">
        <a href="#cite_note-154">
         [153]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Ozone_depletion">
        Ozone depletion
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:NASA_and_NOAA_Announce_Ozone_Hole_is_a_Double_Record_Breaker.png">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="716" data-file-width="716" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/NASA_and_NOAA_Announce_Ozone_Hole_is_a_Double_Record_Breaker.png/220px-NASA_and_NOAA_Announce_Ozone_Hole_is_a_Double_Record_Breaker.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/NASA_and_NOAA_Announce_Ozone_Hole_is_a_Double_Record_Breaker.png/330px-NASA_and_NOAA_Announce_Ozone_Hole_is_a_Double_Record_Breaker.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/NASA_and_NOAA_Announce_Ozone_Hole_is_a_Double_Record_Breaker.png/440px-NASA_and_NOAA_Announce_Ozone_Hole_is_a_Double_Record_Breaker.png 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:NASA_and_NOAA_Announce_Ozone_Hole_is_a_Double_Record_Breaker.png" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Image of the largest Antarctic
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ozone_hole" title="Ozone hole">
          ozone hole
         </a>
         ever recorded due to
         <a href="/wiki/Chlorofluorocarbon" title="Chlorofluorocarbon">
          CFCs
         </a>
         accumulation (September 2006)
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Ozone_depletion" title="Ozone depletion">
        Ozone depletion
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       There is a large area of low ozone concentration or "
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ozone_hole" title="Ozone hole">
        ozone hole
       </a>
       " over Antarctica. This hole covers almost the whole continent and was at its largest in September 2008, when the longest lasting hole on record remained until the end of December.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-155">
        <a href="#cite_note-155">
         [154]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The hole was detected by scientists in 1985
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-schiermeier2009_156-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-schiermeier2009-156">
         [155]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and has tended to increase over the years of observation. The ozone hole is attributed to the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Atmospheric_emissions" title="Atmospheric emissions">
        emission
       </a>
       of
       <a href="/wiki/Chlorofluorocarbon" title="Chlorofluorocarbon">
        chlorofluorocarbons
       </a>
       or CFCs into the atmosphere, which decompose the
       <a href="/wiki/Ozone" title="Ozone">
        ozone
       </a>
       into other gases.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-157">
        <a href="#cite_note-157">
         [156]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Some scientific studies suggest that ozone depletion may have a dominant role in governing climatic change in Antarctica (and a wider area of the Southern Hemisphere).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-schiermeier2009_156-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-schiermeier2009-156">
         [155]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Ozone absorbs large amounts of ultraviolet radiation in the
       <a href="/wiki/Stratosphere" title="Stratosphere">
        stratosphere
       </a>
       . Ozone depletion over Antarctica can cause a cooling of around 6 °C in the local stratosphere. This cooling has the effect of intensifying the westerly winds which flow around the continent (the
       <a href="/wiki/Polar_vortex" title="Polar vortex">
        polar vortex
       </a>
       ) and thus prevents outflow of the cold air near the South Pole. As a result, the continental mass of the East Antarctic ice sheet is held at lower temperatures, and the peripheral areas of Antarctica, especially the Antarctic Peninsula, are subject to higher temperatures, which promote accelerated melting.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-schiermeier2009_156-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-schiermeier2009-156">
         [155]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Models also suggest that the ozone depletion/enhanced polar vortex effect also accounts for the recent increase in sea ice just offshore of the continent.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-158">
        <a href="#cite_note-158">
         [157]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <div style="clear:both;">
      </div>

      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">
        Notes
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="reflist" style="list-style-type: decimal;">
       <div class="mw-references-wrap">
        <ol class="references">
         <li id="cite_note-5">
          <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
           <b>
            <a href="#cite_ref-5">
             ^
            </a>
           </b>
          </span>
          <span class="reference-text">
           The word was originally pronounced without the first
           <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
            /k/
           </span>
           in English, but the
           <a href="/wiki/Spelling_pronunciation" title="Spelling pronunciation">
            spelling pronunciation
           </a>
           has become common and is often considered more correct. The pronunciation without the first /k/ and the first /t/ is however widespread and a typical phenomenon of English in many other similar words too.
           <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2">
            <a href="#cite_note-2">
             [2]
            </a>
           </sup>
           The "c" already ceased to be pronounced in Medieval Latin and was dropped from the spelling in Old French, but it was added back to the spelling for etymological reasons in English in the 17th century and then began to be pronounced, but (as with other spelling pronunciations) at first only by less educated people.
           <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3">
            <a href="#cite_note-3">
             [3]
            </a>
           </sup>
           <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4">
            <a href="#cite_note-4">
             [4]
            </a>
           </sup>
          </span>
         </li>
        </ol>
       </div>
      </div>
